AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Fix alerts for Google calendars on Mac
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: fix-alerts-for-google-calendars-on-mac
CATEGORY: Mac

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2014/02/fix-alerts-for-google-calendars-on-mac.html 
DATE: 02/07/2014 10:32:14 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I like to live life on the edge, and helping me to do that is my Mac&#39;s Calendar app with its predliction for silently discarding all alert settings for Google calendar entries. This stresses me out as I&#39;m just waiting for the first time I embarassingly fail to turn up for something. Today, and frankly not for the first time, I set out on a serious Google hunt to find a resolution. I now have one that&#39;s just about tolerable.</p>
<p>It seems the basic problem is that when you modify a Google calendar entry via Calendar, it then immediately syncs back from Google and wipes out any alert that you just configured. But you didn&#39;t want to go to that meeting anyway, right?</p>
<p>I can&#39;t claim original credit for my solution, but I can endeavour to explain it very clearly and add a few extra tidbits. The trick/workaround is to configure Google Calendar itself, via its web UI, to have a default alert for all newly created events. Then it seems that you can modify the alert successfully from within Calendar, to a different number of minutes ahead. However you can&#39;t remove it, so you have to just live with alerts for everything. To me that&#39;s far preferable to none at all.</p>
<p>You can change the setting for Google Calendar as follows.</p>
<ul>
<li>Sign in to your Google calendar at&#0160;<a href="https://www.google.com/calendar" target="_self">https://www.google.com/calendar</a>.</li>
<li>Open the settings page by selecting &quot;Settings&quot; from the gear dropdown in the top right.</li>
<li>Select the &quot;Calendars&quot; tab at the top.</li>
<li>In the list of calendars (which probably only contains your one calendar) select &quot;Reminders and notifications&quot;</li>
<li>In the &quot;Event reminders&quot; section at the top, select &quot;Add a reminder&quot;</li>
<li>Select &quot;Popup&quot; and however many minutes you want, for the default reminder.</li>
<li>Don&#39;t forget to click the Save button at the top left.</li>
</ul>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: A bit of beer tasting
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: a-bit-of-beer-tasting
CATEGORY: Beer

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2013/11/a-bit-of-beer-tasting.html 
DATE: 11/03/2013 09:05:36 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I made a pilgrimage to <a href="http://beershopstalbans.com" target="_self">BEER:shop St Albans</a>, just on the other side of town, to get some interesting beers. Well why not, and it even had the wife&#39;s blessing because she fancied some good beer. The shop&#39;s only been open for three weeks, but it&#39;s very smart and carries a carefully curated collection. Some of it is really rather pricey, and with those prices on stickers at the back of each bottle it was a slightly frustrating experience. I&#39;d see something interesting, pick it up, confirm that it did indeed sound like a cracker, then turn it around to find it was £15. I could have three bottles of wine for the price of that bottle of beer! But in the end I settled on eight to take home.</p>
<p style="text-align: center;">
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019b00961dce970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BoBSherunkel" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834019b00961dce970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019b00961dce970c-800wi" title="BoBSherunkel" /></a><br /><br /></p>
<p>And now, time to sample a couple of them. First up, an extremely local number from <a href="http://bob-brewery.co.uk" target="_self">The private brewery of BoB</a> which is located only about a hundred metres from the shop as it happens. <strong>BoB Sherunkel</strong> is 3.7%, described as a &quot;delightfully dark mild&quot;. And it really is, with a roasted flavour that lingers on the palette, whilst being incredibly easy drinking. Very pleasant.</p>
<p style="text-align: center;">
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019b00a0cbb9970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BoBSlayah" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834019b00a0cbb9970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019b00a0cbb9970c-800wi" title="BoBSlayah" /></a></p>
<p>Next, Bob Slayah, from the same stable, and thought it would appear to be an entirely different beer (5.2%, &quot;premium bitter&quot;) it had overtones of the same roasty flavour, so it followed on nicely.</p>
<p style="text-align: center;">
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019b00a13958970d-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="EpicSaison" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834019b00a13958970d" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019b00a13958970d-800wi" title="EpicSaison" /></a><br /><br /></p>
<p>And finally, The Wild Beer Co&#39;s Epic Saison, 5% and as crisp, zesty and spicy as the label suggested. Using exotic American hops, it is quite fruity, almost a bit soapy to Mrs C&#39;s tastes. I wouldn&#39;t go out of my way to have another one, but it was perfectly pleasant. I also forgot to photograph it post-pouring. Whoops.</p>
<p>&#0160;</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Paint-proof paint refuses second coat
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: paint-proof-paint-refuses-second-coat
CATEGORY: DIY
CATEGORY: Garden

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2013/08/paint-proof-paint-refuses-second-coat.html 
DATE: 08/28/2013 10:16:15 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019aff0dc6d4970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="CuprinolSecondCoat" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834019aff0dc6d4970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834019aff0dc6d4970b-800wi" style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="CuprinolSecondCoat" /></a></p>
<p>I recently got around to repainting the wooden cabin at the end of my garden and chose <a href="http://www.cuprinol.co.uk/products/garden_shades.jsp" target="_self">Cuprinol&#39;s Garden Shades</a> in delightful Seagrass colour. I discovered something rather unexpected however, that others would be well advised to heed before they embark on a similar mission!</p>
<p>This paint is dead clever and develops a waxy surface in order to repel rainwater and protect your wood. Unfortunately, being a water-based paint, this means it may also repel your attempts to apply a second coat! As you can see in my picture above, it&#39;s like painting on glass and the new paint just beads up into globules as it can&#39;t stick to the previous coat.</p>
<p>It distressed me rather when I discovered this phenomenon and a call to Cuprinol followed as soon as I could look up their number. After a bit of a wait a very knowledgable young lady gave me the lowdown on what was going wrong. She recommended I <strong>rub the surface down with methylated spirits</strong> to break down that waxy layer and I&#39;m glad to say that this did indeed do the trick, though it adds a whole lot of extra faff to the process.</p>
<p>I note that on the can it does say &quot;allow 2-8 hours between coats&quot; and with the benefit of hindsight and the explanation from Cuprinol, I see that this is presumably intended as a range with an upper bound. I had left it for a week, and this allowed that waxy surface to develop, so I should have recoated much sooner. However this text is decidely ambigious and can easily be read as a minimum only, stated as a range due to differing drying times according to conditions. It is especially likely that your average reader will interpret it so, as most paint cans provide just a minimum. Interestingly their website states it a bit differently: &quot;Where a second coat is required, allow up to 8 hours in between coats.&quot; It still neglects to explain why this is, the importance of it, and what to do if you are unable to comply.</p>
<p>So be warned that the innocuous statement on the can is actually very important. Sadly this means you have to set aside enough time in one block to coat everything once, allow it to dry sufficiently then recoat. Otherwise you&#39;ll have to get the meths out. Either way this adds a major inconvenience and the cynic in me suspects that&#39;s why they&#39;re reluctant to explain it properly on the can. The nice lady on the phone did say that they get loads of phone calls about this exact problem and are considering how they can improve the instructions. I want to see a very clear explanation of what happens and why, in a big bold box that draws attention. It should also describe the meths workaround. To do anything less is to fail the customer.</p>
<p>FWIW, I&#39;m now very pleased with the results and will use the product again. I would have been so much happier if I hadn&#39;t had to go through this saga, which involved holding on their expensive-from-a-mobile phone line for ages, and a major expedition to find somewhere with meths in stock.</p>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Equilibrium coding challenge - a Scala solution
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: equilibrium-coding-challenge-a-scala-solution
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Scala
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2013/07/equilibrium-coding-challenge-a-scala-solution.html 
DATE: 07/29/2013 06:07:44 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>Inspired by a random tweet about their added Scala support, I tried out <a href="http://g.codility.com/demo/take-sample-test/" target="_self">the Codility sample test</a>. I rather liked my solution and I think it&#39;s a perfect example of some of the niceties of Scala, in a small way, so here it is:</p>
<pre class="prettyprint">def solution(a: Array[Int]): Int = {
    // Partial sums are Longs to avoid Int overflow.
    val sums = a.scanLeft(0L)(_ + _).tail

    def equilibrium(index: Int) = leftSum(index) == rightSum(index)
    def leftSum(index: Int) = if (index == 0) 0 else sums(index - 1)
    def rightSum(index: Int) = sums.last - sums(index)

    (0 until a.length) find (equilibrium(_)) getOrElse(-1)
}</pre>
The use of scanLeft to build up all the partial sums is particularly handy. Having done that it&#39;s very easy to run through all the indexes until we find one that satisfies us. Note that the find method returns an Option[A] so we use getOrElse to return -1 if no solution was found (as per the requirements).

-----
BODY:
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401910359396f970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="AffligemBlonde" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401910359396f970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401910359396f970c-300wi" style="width: 300px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="AffligemBlonde" /></a><br />When I left a job recently, my Belgian colleagues presented me with a basket filled with a vast and varied array of beers. My arms having recovered from carrying it home, it&#39;s now time to sample these fine wares.</p>
<p>First up Affligem Blonde, at 6.8% a relative lightweight in the Belgian canon, but I was impressed by the overall result. A bit like Leffe, but more subtle and probably less cloying if you were to drain a few. It&#39;s really smooth and well-balanced, just edging only slightly into the treacly tendencies of classic Belgian brews, but definitely going beyond &quot;straightforward lager&quot;. I really liked it - it&#39;s a great beer to start an evening of lowland delicacies or for general consumption if you&#39;re not feeling like the heavy stuff.</p>
<p>Expect more over the coming weeks, and expect them to be mostly in entirely the wong glasses, as above.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Windows 8 = CQRS
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: windows-8-cqrs
CATEGORY: Culture
CATEGORY: Design
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Me
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2013/04/windows-8-cqrs.html 
DATE: 04/20/2013 11:21:54 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401901b712355970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="CQRSLaptopTablet" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401901b712355970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401901b712355970b-800wi" style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="CQRSLaptopTablet" /></a><br />Allow me to wax philosphical for a moment with an observation about where computers and their operating systems are heading.</p>
<p>In the world of software development CQRS = Command Query Responsibility Segregation, which in its simplest sense recognises that it&#39;s sometimes better to&#0160;use a different mechanism for reading data than it is for writing it.&#0160;<a href="http://martinfowler.com/bliki/CQRS.html" target="_self">See Martin Fowler&#39;s exposition of the concept</a>&#0160;if you want to know more, but&#0160;this post isn&#39;t actually about software development at all!</p>
<p><strong>I reckon that <strong>we&#39;re at a critical juncture in the evolution of personal computing devices and that&#0160;</strong>the CQRS principle is necessarily coming to the fore to save the human race.</strong></p>
<p>Tablet computers are taking the world by storm, in case you hadn&#39;t noticed. Apple could barely make enough iPad Minis for me to be able to get my wife one for Xmas, though I did manage it at the very last minute, and shortly thereafter bagged one for myself too. Frankly it&#39;s bloody brilliant, but I use it predominantly for consuming rather than creating and I&#39;m far from alone. This is partly because the human populace is inexorably dumbing down towards being fat blobs with brains wired directly into the &#39;net, consuming inane banter, amusing picture of cats&#0160;and the latest celebrity news, 140 characters at a time. But that aside, it&#39;s just not very pleasant to write large quantities of text, manipulate images or perform other expansive creative works by prodding a tiny screen. Or even a big screen.</p>
<p>To write software, construct lengthy blog posts (ahem), edit movies, sequence the human genome or design great buildings requires a proper computer! On that basis I posit that there will always be a place for desktops and laptops, or indeed whatever replaces them but which necessarily has a non-trivial input mechanism.&#0160;I genuinely worry that the market for serious computers will be increasingly neglected by the manufacturers, refocussing as they are on the mass consumer market, inevitably leading to the downfall of humankind. Perhaps I exaggerate - at least I hope so.</p>
<p>Now I&#39;ve never used Windows 8, indeed I shudder at having to use Windows 7 on a daily basis at work, but I understand it represents something of a chimera. It is best known for its shiny, touchy, slidey &#39;Metro&#39; UI, beckoning your greasy fingers to caress its tiles. However it also allows you to fall back into the more staid world of traditional Windows where presumably you can get some proper work done, as long as you have a keyboard and a pointing device other than your finger. I understand critics are conflicted about this hybrid approach, but it&#39;s CQRS writ large and may therefore be the way forwards. One way or another, at least some people will need to create great works. I do hope to be one of them, and to have the equipment to be able to do it.</p>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Making a brick path in my garden
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: making-a-brick-path-in-my-garden
CATEGORY: DIY
CATEGORY: Garden
CATEGORY: Me

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2013/04/making-a-brick-path-in-my-garden.html 
DATE: 04/14/2013 10:00:49 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a3e7970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 2" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a3e7970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a3e7970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 2" /></a></p>
<p>I have a fair amount of lawn in my garden, which is looking pretty ropey right now, but I&#39;m working on it! However I&#39;m intending to convert a lot of it to more intriguing beds and paths. This project details one little part of that grand plan. In the picture above, I&#39;ve removed the turf from a corner, which originally had just a narrow bed at the back against the trellis.</p>
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8bc6970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 3" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8bc6970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8bc6970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 3" /></a></p>
<p>My plan was to put a curving brick path through this new area, leaving an eye-shaped island that will probably host a small tree and other planting high enough and rumbustious enough to make the path a little voyage of discovery. A very little voyage, but adding much-needed intrigue.</p>
<p>I started by using the garden hose to experiment with the shape of the path, using some bricks to get the width right. What a beautiful shape!</p>
<p>Those bricks, and indeed all the bricks I will use, are from the rear chimney on the house, which was removed in a recent loft conversion (the chimney, not the house). They are a mix of old handmade red clay bricks and nasty moulded modern things, but they have some character, including sooty marks, and I think mixed up they&#39;re going to look the part.</p>
<p>I&#39;m not sure how well they will survive the harsh winters that seem to be the new normal. I strongly suspect&#0160;they will suffer from frost damage and it will be interesting to find out whether it&#39;s the old or new that perform better. I have plenty left to repair the damage. For the record, ideally you&#39;d use engineering bricks, but I don&#39;t have any and I feel it would lack the charm of a crumbling old brick path.</p>
<div>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a46c970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 4" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a46c970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a46c970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 4" /></a></div>
<p>Having established the basic shape and scored a mark in the soil with the edge of a spade along the hosepipe, I dug it out about a brick deep plus a couple of centimetres for the sand. I also added 5cm or so on either side to give room for manoeuvre.</p>
<p>Theoretically I should use pegs in the ground, string and spirit levels to establish a perfectly level, flat path, but that&#39;s just not my style, and the two ends are not at the same level anyway. I used a couple of bits of straight wood and my eye to make sure that the soil bed was basically level with no major local bumps or hollows. Again I invoke &#39;rustic&#39; as an excuse for my slapdashery.</p>
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a512970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 5" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a512970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017d42c9a512970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 5" /></a></p>
<p>Do you know why sharp sand is called sharp sand? Because the individual grains are all knobbly and &#39;sharp&#39;, compared to non-sharp sand which has smooth, rounded grains. This helps them to lock together and form a firm base. I&#39;ve used four bags of sharp sand and with the aid of a rake and a piece of wood the width of the path dragged over it, I&#39;ve turned it into a smooth base ready for my bricks. I spent quite a while trying to get this right, as it will be reflected in the final result.</p>
<p>By the way, I bought six bags of sand from Wickes for the grand total of £10.86, and that was my entire spend on this project. The rest is bricks and tools I already had, and my own labour.</p>
<p><a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8d13970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 1" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8d13970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8d13970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 1" /></a></p>
<p>I figured it was best to lay one whole run the length of the path, to establish and tweak the basic curve. Then of course the subsequent runs are a bit longer each time around the curve, so we need half-bricks here and there to keep a good pattern going.</p>
<p>I used a bolster chisel and club hammer to cut the bricks by hand, which takes a bit of technique, a certain amount of practise and a lot of patience, especially when it&#39;s raining and you can&#39;t see through your goggles (you don&#39;t want brick chips in your eye). I think most people would find this the most challenging part of the project if they haven&#39;t done it before. You can get machines to do it nicely and with no effort on your part, which would be worthwhile if doing anything much bigger. A straight path would only require half-bricks at the ends of course.</p>
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8da1970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 6" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8da1970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8da1970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 6" /></a></p>
<p>Once I&#39;d got all the bricks down it looked pretty good, but only then did I fork over the beds either side, being very careful not to disturb my new path. I had been agonising over whether to do that first or last. I feared that if I did it first I might loosen the firm bed for my path, compacted nicely as it was under the old lawn. Also, it would have made working around the path and seeing the edges a lot harder, so I&#39;m glad I did it this way around.</p>
<p>
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8de7970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrickPath 7" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8de7970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017c389a8de7970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="BrickPath 7" /></a></p>
<p>Finally, I did that digging of the compacted soil, and packed it up to the edge of my bricks, ready to receive plants. Then I brushed more sharp sand into all of the cracks to finish off the path itself. That took a surprisingly long time and a few goes as the bricks settled and new holes opened up. Using very dry sand really helps it to fall down those cracks so I dried mine out in the sun before using it.</p>
<p>I&#39;m very pleased with the result, but time will tell whether it&#39;s really any good. Next project is the planting either side of it.</p>
-----

TITLE: Beer review: Gordon Finest Gold
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 0
BASENAME: beer-review-gordon-finest-gold
CATEGORY: Beer
CATEGORY: Travel

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2013/03/beer-review-gordon-finest-gold.html 
DATE: 03/17/2013 10:23:10 PM
-----
BODY:
<p style="text-align: center;">&#0160;
<a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017ee974512f970d-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="GordonFinestGold" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834017ee974512f970d" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834017ee974512f970d-400wi" style="width: 400px;" title="GordonFinestGold" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">I do love a Belgian beer. I don&#39;t know how they do it, but their strong, sweet creations are the beery equivalent of comfort food to me. But this is a strange creature that I review today.</p>
<p>Bought in the Carrefour Express in Brussels Midi station, it appears to be a slice of Scottishness amid the Belgian fancies, certainly in terms of the branding. But it&#39;s 10% by volume and is quite clearly a deliciously sticky Belgian tipple exactly as I would expect, and decidedly un-Scottish. I suppose Barley wine may be the connection. It&#39;s described on the bottle as &quot;strong blond and mellow beer&quot; and I can&#39;t argue with that.</p>
<p>Is this just a piece of branding silliness, selling a bit of tartan-clad och-aye-the-noo to the rest of the world, who do appear to love that sort of thing? Possibly. But I actually liked it so much that I&#39;d buy it again.</p>
<p>For what it&#39;s worth a little delve into its provenance takes us to anthonymartin.com where we find that an English brewer setled in Belgium and started producing beers in 1909 and that this particular brew has been around since 1990 and they own the TImmermans brewery known for Gueuze Lambics.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Detail consultant
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: detail-consultant
CATEGORY: Design
CATEGORY: Me
CATEGORY: Travel
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2012/06/detail-consultant.html 
DATE: 06/17/2012 10:44:59 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><a class="asset-img-link" href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340176159864c8970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Detail" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340176159864c8970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340176159864c8970c-800wi" style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="Detail" /></a><br />I see irritating problems. Every tangible thing I use, every service I consume, I can&#39;t help but notice all the little things that could have been a bit better. And it really frustrates me when these are details that seem so very simple to fix.</p>
<p>For instance, when I buy a train ticket from FCC&#39;s machines at St Pancras I can&#39;t actually see the text on the card payment screen unless I squat uncomfortably, because it&#39;s low down and inset out of sight. I know it&#39;s low down so disabled/short people can use it, but at least provide a direct line of sight for the average sized person standing in front of the machine - i.e. 95% of your customers!</p>
<p>I recently bought a D-Link powerline networking kit on Amazon, which is a wonderful product by the way, that worked straight out of the box with zero configuration. But the Amazon page did not state that there were two ethernet cables in the box - though I suspected there might be and was explicitly looking for this information. Worse than that, it provided a handy link to buy the product along with two separately supplied ethernet cables. So I went with that (better safe than sorry) but lo and behold: two cables in the box and now I have two extras I paid for and don&#39;t need. I feel I have been poorly served by Amazon.</p>
<p>Often when travelling by train, announcements are made that are inaudible due to the low volume (compared to background noise) or muffled by unhelpful acoustics. But these are sometimes really very important announcements and there&#39;s precious little point making them if they can&#39;t be heard.</p>
<p>Whenever I visit the hairdresser they ask me what I want doing, and I struggle to articulate it, especially when it comes to the intricacies of the back of my head. Why don&#39;t they take photos and notes (in special hairdresser shorthand) so that they can sort me out consistently every time, even when it&#39;s somebody new cutting my hair. As far as I&#39;m concerned this is a no-brainer.</p>
<p>There are many little disappointments just like this that I spot on a daily basis, with physical and virtual products and services and I&#39;m increasingly wondering whether there&#39;s a way to turn this into a job: detail consultant. Available for hire by companies small and large, I would tell them what could be improved to give their customers a delightful rather than frustrating experience. It&#39;s not rocket science but apparently there&#39;s a gap for this sort of common sense supplied from an external viewpoint. Please form an orderly queue!</p>
<p>In fact I&#39;m surprised that larger organisations don&#39;t have full time employee roles with exactly this mandate: roving throughout the company and in the field spotting and fixing these subtle issues. In a competitive world where bad publicity is only a Tweet away, this seems like a very good use of resources to my naive mind. If you have any pride at all in what you&#39;re doing that is.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Apache Bench (ab) stalls after 16000 requests, due to ephemeral ports
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: apache-bench-ab-stalls-after-16000-requests-ephemeral-ports
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Play 2
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Scala
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2012/05/apache-bench-ab-stalls-after-16000-requests-ephemeral-ports.html 
DATE: 05/04/2012 10:32:48 AM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve been doing some trivial benchmarking of <a href="http://www.playframework.org/" target="_self">Play 2</a> with ab (<a href="http://httpd.apache.org/docs/2.0/programs/ab.html" target="_self">Apache Bench</a>) just to get an idea of its raw capabilities for serving simple requests - and because it&#39;s what I always do when picking up a new framework so I know what I&#39;m dealing with. In doing so I ran into a bit of a puzzler that had me thinking Play 2 was bugged - but my spidey sense soon kicked in and told me it was more likely to be an OS or ab issue. I had done approximately the following, using Play 2.0.1 on OS X 10.7.3, and I&#39;m pretty certain you&#39;ll see the same results if you do this on a Mac:</p>
<pre><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; play new hello  [select option 1 - basic Scala app]</span><br /><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; cd hello</span><br /><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; play start</span><br /><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; ab -c 50 -n 16000 http://localhost:9000/  [Runs fine - about 3700rps]</span><br /><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; ab -c 50 -n 16000 http://localhost:9000/  [Gives up with timeout]</span><br /><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; ab -c 50 -n 16000 http://localhost:9000/  [Runs fine - about 3700rps]</span><br /><span style="font-size: 10pt;">&gt; ab -c 50 -n 16000 http://localhost:9000/  [Gives up with timeout]</span></pre>
<p>It took me a bit of experimentation to establish that it&#39;s about 16000 requests that work fine, followed by timeouts, in a reliable pattern. That&#39;s a suspicious number, being near enough a power of 2, which is what clued me into it being an OS limit that I was running into. I ran the same ab test (with the same result) against the built in Apache https serving a static file, confirming that Play 2 probably wasn&#39;t to blame.</p>
<p>Sure enough, a quick Google turns up the goods. My OS was running out of the approximately 16000 ephemeral ports available and having to wait for them to be released before it could reuse them. So not Play 2 or ab&#39;s fault at all. Actually in some senses it <em>is</em> Play 2&#39;s fault for being so fast that I&#39;ve run into this limit.</p>
<p>I&#39;m not going to go into the details of <a href="http://www.ncftp.com/ncftpd/doc/misc/ephemeral%5Fports.html" target="_self">what ephemeral ports really are</a>, as others have done that perfectly well, and there is a <a href="http://stackoverflow.com/a/1217100" target="_self">good StackOverflow answer</a> with some key ways to work around the problem by modifying parameters of the OS&#39; network stack - but be careful and make sure you understand what you&#39;re doing.</p>
<p>However, one very simple way to workaround the issue is to simply pass the -k option to ab, to use HTTP keepalive (assuming the server you&#39;re testing supports it). Note that this changes the nature of your test though, as you&#39;re no longer really simulating large numbers of separate connections - but for basic sanity check testing it may help. For the record `ab -c 50 -n 100000 -k http://localhost:9000/` benchmarked&#0160;Play 2 at about 7000 requests per second on my 2.4GHz Core Duo MacBook.</p>
-----
EXTENDED BODY:

-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Getting my feet wet with Scala
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: getting-my-feet-wet-with-scala
CATEGORY: Groovy / Grails
CATEGORY: Play 2
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Scala
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2012/04/getting-my-feet-wet-with-scala.html 
DATE: 04/01/2012 10:41:49 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>The hype around the&#0160;<a href="http://www.scala-lang.org/" target="_self">Scala</a>&#0160;programming language just got too much recently and I decided to give it a go. I&#39;m two thirds of the way through&#0160;<a href="http://www.artima.com/shop/programming_in_scala" target="_self">Programming in Scala</a>, a massive but very good tome and I&#39;ve been experimenting with the language and tools, though only a little bit so far. I have also just received the newly published&#0160;<a href="http://typesafe.com/resources/scala-for-the-impatient">Scala for the Impatient</a>&#0160;(a deliberately much more compact book) and will be working through that as well.</p>
<p>To put it mildly, Scala is not for the faint hearted, or anyone who just wants to get some stuff done ASAP. There is a big, steep learning curve and the relative immaturity of the language and the small community means you&#39;d better be used to the pains of the bleeding edge. I thought Ruby was hard work, but Scala is frankly more so in my experience so far.</p>
<p>However the language is somewhat addictive - or perhaps I just like the challenge of learning stuff that takes a bit of grokking. It&#39;s a big language, with many very clever facilities and features. It&#39;s mind-boggling to start with but I think it&#39;s starting to sink in. Of course there&#39;s the functional paradigm to understand and master, but I did plenty of that at university so it doesn&#39;t frighten me.</p>
<p>Enough people have written about the&#0160;<a href="http://zeroturnaround.com/blog/scala-sink-or-swim-part-1/">frustrations</a>&#0160;<a href="http://zeroturnaround.com/blog/scala-sink-or-swim-part-2/">and</a>&#0160;<a href="http://zeroturnaround.com/blog/scala-sink-or-swim-part-3/">wonders</a>&#0160;of writing code with Scala, so I think I will reflect on a few miscellaneous findings, mostly of a practical bent.</p>
<h3>Compiler speed</h3>
<p>The Scala compiler is surprisingly slow and it seems to be one of the main bitching points amongst people trying to get up to speed with the language. But seriously, when you&#39;re used to instantaneous results with Grails, or even plain old Java (especially with JRebel) then waiting several seconds even for a trivial app is most upsetting. But I&#39;m trying to be Zen about it.</p>
<p>The mailing lists tend to be full of griping about the compiler&#39;s speed, usually met with promises that it&#39;s getting faster, but the Scala compiler is so much more complicated than the Java compiler that it seems unlikely to do its work in the blink of an eye anytime soon. See&#0160;<a href="http://stackoverflow.com/a/3612212" target="_self">Martin Odersky&#39;s explanation of why it&#39;s slow</a>&#0160;on Stack Overflow.</p>
<h3>Eclipse support</h3>
<p>Eclipse supposedly has good Scala support via the Scala-IDE plugin, but I found the out of box experience so terrible that I have given up. Having created a &quot;new Scala project&quot; I still had to manually add the Scala libraries to the project and manually create run configurations, which would very often still not appear in the lists of ways to run the project. I just want to hit &quot;Go&quot; and for my app to run - it should be trivially easy to make this happen. In my experience the best open source projects are the ones that deliver a delightful out-of-box experience and this has disappointed me on that front.</p>
<h3>IntelliJ IDEA support</h3>
<p>Luckily the Community (i.e. free) edition of IDEA supports Scala. Even though I hadn&#39;t used IntelliJ previously, I figured out how to download their Scala plugin and create a Scala app within just a couple of minutes and it was a very smooth experience compared to installing the Scala support into Eclipse (don&#39;t get me started).</p>
<p>However compilation of a Hello World app took 7 seconds every time I modified the single file! A bit of Googling led me to discover FSC - the Fast Scala Compiler - which is part of the standard Scala toolset. Support for FSC is built-in to IDEA but that support is turned off by default. Once I turned it on for my project things hotted up and compilation took just 2 seconds. That&#39;s still lamentable compared to most other languages, but just about tolerable for now. I have no idea how things go for a big project, though I get the impression from mailing lists that waiting tens of seconds or even minutes for a compile is fairly commonplace. We shall see.</p>
<h3>Play 2.0</h3>
<p>One of the reasons I decided to try Scala in the first place was the fuss over the&#0160;<a href="http://www.playframework.org/" target="_self">Play 2.0 framework</a>, which has just recently been released in its first &#39;complete&#39; form. I have only messed with a couple of tutorials so far and frankly it&#39;s bewildering and strange coming from frameworks like Spring MVC, Grails, and Ramaze (a small Ruby framework).</p>
<p>There is much wailing and gnashing of teeth on the Play mailing list from people upset about its radical new direction, and its emphasis on Scala compared to Play 1. Maybe it&#39;s just the pain of change, but there&#39;s no doubt Play 2 is hard work to get to grips with.</p>
<p>I get the impression that a lot of people are missing the point though - it&#39;s a case of horses for courses. Play 2 is rather exotically architected using cunning non-blocking approaches that require it to abandon the classic Java Servlet container entirely. It also requires you to write obtuse and sometimes verbose code (compared to Grails say - though it&#39;s usually less verbose than Java) and partly because of that non-blocking architecture. Reading and understanding the&#0160;<a href="http://www.playframework.org/documentation/2.0/ScalaActionsComposition" target="_self">Play 2 docs on Action Composition</a>&#0160;may very well require a PhD, but Action Composition is a technique that must be used to achieve relatively common ends. It&#39;s all a bit overwhelming for newbies.</p>
<p>This clever shenanigans enables it to handle 40,000 requests per second (albeit very very simple requests) using less than 20MB RAM. I saw Guillame Bort demonstrate this at QCon and it&#39;s certainly impressive. The point though is that it&#39;s all architected for the sort of new-breed web app that&#39;s dealing with connected rich-clients rapidly pushing and pulling data. If you want to create a few CRUD pages for a small admin team to look after a database then I very much doubt that Play 2 is for you.</p>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Three, is the magic number
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: three-is-the-magic-number
CATEGORY: iPhone

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2012/01/three-is-the-magic-number.html 
DATE: 01/26/2012 10:39:48 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I recently switched from O2 to Three (whilst picking up a new iPhone 4S) and frankly it’s great. It must be great if I&#39;m blogging about it! Key wins are as follows.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>All you can eat data</strong> with Three. O2 have recently abandoned unlimited data and charge based on fixed price data packages for paltry amounts – e.g. 500MB for £6 per month. To be fair I should point out that their systems told me I was only using about 200MB per month previously. But read on…</li>
<li><strong>Tethering! </strong>I can turn on Personal Hotspot on my iPhone and connect to it via Bluetooth, USB or WiFi from another computer and get all the wonders of the internet. This has revolutionised my train ride to work with my laptop. Phone stays in pocket and via Bluetooth I’m fully connected to the world. This is technically possible on some other networks but often only at a price, either to turn it on at all, or because you’ll be charged oodles for the data you use up.</li>
<li><strong>Raw speed</strong>. Over 3G I now regularly get 8MBit downstream, 1.5MBit upstream. That’s better than many people’s home broadband and a lot better than I ever got with O2.</li>
</ul>
<p>It&#39;s not all fun and sunshine though. I found a couple of major downsides, which I will just have to live with.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Visual voicemail doesn’t exist on Three. </strong>Frankly I was dumb-founded when I noticed this. I literally couldn&#39;t believe it, but apparently it&#39;s true.&#0160;Instead you get a text message telling you that you have a voicemail and then have to phone a voicemail number and painfully navigate through things with the numeric keypad. It’s positively medieval and if I wasn&#39;t so smitten with their data service it would definitely be a reason to avoid Three entirely.<br /><br />Of course you only find this out after you&#39;ve signed up because it&#39;s not something they point out to you. I have had conflicting messages from different Three employees in shops and on the customer service line about whether they ever intend to get with the program here. Alas I fear they won&#39;t.</li>
<li><strong>Customer service can be dodgy.</strong> It was a struggle to get my phone up and running in the first place due to some miscommunication and dodgy information from Three. Their customer service line is like a choose your own adventure book - consisting of endless numeric options designed to avoid having to actually talk to you. Once I actually got to speak to somebody it was alright, though I had to fight my way through the number-maze each time - they wouldn&#39;t give me a direct number.<br /><br />Before my phone was activated (which is ironically what I was trying to phone up to arrange) it was literally impossible to talk to someone because the first auto-question asked for your mobile number and then complained that it wasn&#39;t activated! Apparently you have to type hash to defeat this particular monster but <strong>they didn&#39;t tell me that and the prompt doesn&#39;t mention it</strong>. Apparently it&#39;s a secret.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
</ul>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Blocks!
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: blocks
CATEGORY: DIY
CATEGORY: Me

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2011/11/blocks.html 
DATE: 11/13/2011 10:51:31 PM
-----
BODY:

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Grails custom exception mapping
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: grails-custom-exception-mapping
CATEGORY: Groovy / Grails
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2011/03/grails-custom-exception-mapping.html 
DATE: 03/07/2011 10:40:51 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><strong>Updated 17/3/2011 with a solution to Spring Security redirecting</strong>.</p>
<p>I had a bit of a fight against Grails to get security exceptions handled the way I wanted, but having figured it out I thought I&#39;d write it up. It&#39;s really very simple - I had just had the wrong end of the stick.</p>
<p>I want to be able to throw an org.springframework.security.access.AccessDeniedException from anywhere in my code and have that appear to the user as a custom error page of my design, but with a 403 response code (that&#39;s 403 Forbidden, rather than 500 Internal Server Error) and maintaining the originally requested URL rather than redirecting to an error URL as Spring Security sometimes prefers to do (because then you can&#39;t refresh to retry).</p>
<p>My main confusion arose from the way Grails&#39; UrlMappings.groovy handles custom exception mapping. It turns out that you need to do it like this:</p>
<pre>// Handling specific exceptions requires a 500 code on the left for the
// mapping to pick them up, but we can send back another code in the
// controller that sends back the response.
&quot;500&quot;(controller:&quot;error&quot;, action:&quot;error403&quot;, exception:AccessDeniedException)</pre>
<p>The only surprising thing is that you need a &quot;500&quot; code at the left rather than &quot;403&quot;. The reasoning seems to be that this is the <em>incoming</em> error code to the mapping function, and <em>all</em> exceptions are deemed to represent a 500 error code at this point, i.e. they represent an internal server error, which on reflection isn&#39;t entirely unreasonable. To then send the error back to the user I have an ErrorController and an associated view in /views/error/error403.gsp.</p>
<pre>@Secured([&#39;permitAll&#39;])
class ErrorController {
    def error403 = {
        // Ensure that the correct response code gets sent. If we don&#39;t do
        // this, it may send a 500 (internal server error) response because
        // of the way we had to configure the UrlMappings for handling specific
        // exception types with 500.
        return response.sendError(javax.servlet.http.HttpServletResponse.SC_FORBIDDEN)
    }
}</pre>
<p>I also figured out how to stop Spring redirecting to a new URL when it decided to deny access. I&#39;d rather the originally requested URL remain in the address bar. The trick is to put the following in Config.groovy.</p>
<pre>grails.plugins.springsecurity.adh.errorPage = null</pre>
<p>The major remaining annoyance is that these exceptions get reported in the logs whereas I&#39;d rather they weren&#39;t (they represent the system working correctly) but I imagine I have to reconfigure logging to resolve that.</p>

TITLE: Grails dependency hell
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: grails-dependency-hell
CATEGORY: Groovy / Grails
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/12/grails-dependency-hell.html 
DATE: 12/24/2010 02:33:10 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I just upgraded to latest Spock and Geb releases (0.5 and 0.5.1 respectively) but have had to undergo a strange sort of hell of odd errors before getting back on track. Turned out none of these were due to any actual code incompatibilities and eventually (after a couple of hours of fruitless head scratching) I got past them by running &quot;grails clean&quot; (which got me to some new errors) and finally by deleting ~/ivy2/cache. The latter step sadly seems to be required quite often when adding or upgrading plugins.</p>
<p>Personally I&#39;m bitterly disappointed that Ivy (or Maven, if that&#39;s your poison), the so-called solutions to dependency hell, seems to introduce its own dependency nightmare more often than not. Of course every time you delete that Ivy cache folder you have to wait fifteen minutes the next time you run the app, whilst it downloads hundreds of jars. If you&#39;re on the train and not connected to t&#39;Internet, you&#39;re really stuck.</p>
<p>I&#39;m sure one day I&#39;ll grok what&#39;s really going on with this stuff, but for now I feel it ought to just work, but it really doesn&#39;t, and many people must waste a lot of time (or give up entirely) as a result.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Terrible Siemens fridge controls
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: terrible-siemens-fridge-controls
CATEGORY: Design
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/11/terrible-siemens-fridge-controls.html 
DATE: 11/02/2010 09:43:59 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>The problem with a lot of industrial design is that it&#39;s cynically conducted to sell the device in the first place, rather than to actually be any good in the long run. That is to say, the device is designed to appeal to the shop-floor browser who&#39;s making a snap judgement between competing products, rather than to actually be a brilliant product for them to use year after year. Once they&#39;ve bought it the game is over as far as the manufacturer is concerned, as long as it doesn&#39;t actually break down, because poor usability does not invoke the warranty! I came across a great example of this recently in the control panel of a Siemens fridge. Behold:</p>
<p><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340133f58913cf970b-pi" style="display: inline;"> <a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340133f589150b970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="SiemensFridge" border="0" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340133f589150b970b" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340133f589150b970b-800wi" style="display: block; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;" title="SiemensFridge" /></a></a></p>
<p>There is one button on here that you will use several times a day. The rest you may use either never or once every year or two. Care to hazard a guess which one is the magic button of actual usefulness? It&#39;s the one marked &quot;dispenser&quot;. Or to be precise it&#39;s the one marked &quot;dis-penser&quot; split over two lines, such is their lack of grace. This button switches the ice/water dispenser between water, ice and crushed ice. To get a glass of iced water, you have to press it a few times to cycle round the options as you fill your glass with ice, then water.</p>
<p>This should be the only button on the front of the fridge immediately above the dispenser, and it should be very clearly labelled. In fact arguably there should be three buttons, for water, ice and crushed ice, with LEDs to show which one is selected. They should be labelled &quot;Cold water&quot;, &quot;Ice&quot; and &quot;Crushed Ice&quot;. Imagine a visitor to your house approaching your Siemens fridge for a cool drink. They will probably accidentally defrost your freezer then child-lock the fridge before giving up and having tap water. Maybe it&#39;s an eco feature?</p>
<p>The controls that are not related to the water/ice dispenser should be far away from it, possibly inside the fridge door or behind a panel, so as not to pointlessly engender confusion and accidental fridge reconfiguration when trying to get a refreshing beverage.</p>
<p>So why have Siemens chosen to take this insane path? I don&#39;t believe they could be so obviously dumb, so I assume it&#39;s a cynical ploy to sell more fridges. Imagine Joe Shopper, strolling through the aisles of Currys, looking at endless gleaming monoliths of cool technology. Joe wants a high-end fridge, the sort of fridge that says &quot;Joe is a classy guy who won&#39;t let anything stand between him and a glass of brain-freezingly cold water.&quot; Given two stainless steel slabs of Germanic engineering with largely identical specs he&#39;s going to go for the one with the most &#39;impressive&#39; control panel. So actually Joe, it&#39;s your fault, but the nice guys in the Siemens design department must cry themselves to sleep at night.</p>
-----

TITLE: STS 2.5 RC1 "Refreshing external folders" error
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: sts-25-rc1-refreshing-external-folders-error
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/10/sts-25-rc1-refreshing-external-folders-error.html 
DATE: 10/15/2010 08:59:51 PM
-----
BODY:
<p style="text-align: left;">I feverishly installed the latest Springsource Tool Suite release today - 2.5 RC1. Unfortunately it fairly swiftly started giving me the error shown below as I worked on my Grails project. It was popping up really frequently and would be a show-stopper if I couldn&#39;t fix it.</p>
<p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834013488392eab970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Screen shot 2010-10-15 at 20.50.43" class="asset  asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834013488392eab970c" src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834013488392eab970c-400wi" style="width: 400px;" title="Screen shot 2010-10-15 at 20.50.43" /></a></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">It became apparent that it does this every time I saved a Groovy file that required my running Grails app to reload. It seems that STS didn&#39;t like the files in the &#39;target&#39; folder changing as it recompiled, especially since I have &#0160;auto-refresh turned on in STS. I worked around this by setting up a rule for my project to exclude the target folder.</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Right-click on the project, selected &quot;Properties...&quot; then Resource &gt; Resource Filters. Add a filter to exclude the folder &quot;target&quot;.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Groovy and Grails thoughts
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: groovy-and-grails-thoughts
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/08/groovy-and-grails-thoughts.html 
DATE: 08/04/2010 10:30:11 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve been slowly banging away at a small Grails app on the train to and from work, to teach myself <a href="http://groovy.codehaus.org/" target="_blank">Groovy</a> the language and <a href="http://www.grails.org/" target="_blank">Grails</a> the web framework. I already had a good familiarity with Java, Spring and Hibernate so it is mostly a case of learning what Groovy and Grails add on top of those. I&#39;ve still got a long way to go, but I thought it worth jotting down some thoughts in case they help somebody else work their way into this particular world.</p><p>Overall, it&#39;s been fairly pleasant and I&#39;m optimistic that it&#39;s probably as good a way to go about your average web app as any I&#39;ve found before, and I&#39;ve tried plenty over the past decade, including a few fairly obscure ones. I should point out that I don&#39;t believe in silver bullets any more. There are always going to be frustrations along the way when using any technology for a non-trivial project involving real-world customer requirements, but it&#39;s instructive to see just how many of those frustrations there are, and how hard they are to get past. I&#39;m pleased to say that each time I&#39;ve got a bit stuck with Grails I&#39;ve been able to dig myself out fairly swiftly with the help of the existing documentation, other web resources, the #grails channel on freenode IRC and the grails-user mailing list. Furthermore, the principle of least surprise (POLS) is alive and well, meaning that when I hope that I&#39;d find a particular feature to solve my problem du jour, it&#39;s usually there for me to find.</p><p>It&#39;s not all sunshine and roses though. For instance when I upgraded to Grails 1.3.3 my unit tests started failing with a strange and unhelpful stack trace emanating deep from within the guts of the plugin framework. Turns out this is a bug introduced in 1.3.3 that plenty of people have been caught out by. I&#39;ve had to go back to 1.3.2 for now. It&#39;s a shade surprising and disappointing that a release could contain a bug like this, and that it&#39;s not been fixed and re-released yet. I worry that people trying Grails out for the first time with 1.3.3 will have a bad experience and give up early because of issues like this.</p><p>I had a struggle to find half decent tools. I&#39;ve been a huge fan of Netbeans over the past several years and it claims to have great Groovy and Grails support, but I found it to be woeful I&#39;m afraid. Common GSP tags completely confused the GSP editor (it claims the syntax is wrong) so I struggle to believe that those claiming it has this great support have actually tried developing a non-toy Grails app with it. I&#39;ve settled on SpringSource Tool Suite (a bastardised Eclipse) which isn&#39;t bad - though as of this writing you definitely want to get the 2.3.3 M2 version for the latest Groovy and Grails support that&#39;s not yet in the stable release.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Grails GSP HTML escaping confusion
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: grails-gsp-html-escaping-confusion
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/06/grails-gsp-html-escaping-confusion.html 
DATE: 06/30/2010 11:58:17 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;m currently getting to grips with Groovy and Grails. One major frustration that took a while to figure out was HTML escaping in GSP pages. Here&#39;s the simple lowdown, including at the end the important bit that I had struggled to realise, which is how to <strong><em>not</em></strong> escape specific strings when global HTML escaping is turned on.</p>

<h3>Manual escaping</h3>

<p>Out of the box (i.e. with a vanilla Grails app with default config) you need to explicitly escape any dangerous strings with the encodeAsHTML() function that Grails makes available on all Strings: ${dangerString.encodeAsHTML()}. This is a bit verbose, but at least it&#39;s very clear, and because it&#39;s just a method on String it&#39;s available everywhere in your app, not just in GSPs.</p>

<h3>Auto-escaping with default codec</h3>

<p>If you modify Config.groovy to contain&#0160;grails.views.default.codec = &quot;html&quot; (which is there by default and set to &quot;none&quot;) then it automatically calls encodeAsHTML() for you whenever you use ${} in GSPs. This is clearly quite a handy option and a much safer way of configuring things as it lessens the likelihood of slipping up and leaving a hole in your app.</p>

<h3>Overriding auto-escaping per item</h3>

<p>So far this is all exactly as per <a href="http://grails.org/doc/latest/guide/single.html#11.2%20Encoding%20and%20Decoding%20Objects" target="_blank">the Grails docs</a> (which go into much more detail on codecs and what&#39;s really going on, including creating your own) but the crucial bit they fail to mention is what to do if you&#39;ve turned on the global html codec, but have situations where you don&#39;t want escaping. The answer is to simply use the alternative JSP style interpolation syntax &lt;%=mySafeHTMLString%&gt; since the codec is only applied to ${}.</p>

<h3>Overriding auto-escaping per page</h3>
<p>You can also set the codec on a per-page basis, overriding that set in Config.groovy, with&#0160;&lt;%@page defaultCodec=&quot;html&quot; %&gt; or&#0160;&lt;%@page defaultCodec=&quot;none&quot; %&gt; as appropriate.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Beer Review: Clausthaler Low Alcohol Lager
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: beer-review-clausthaler-low-alcohol-lager
CATEGORY: Beer
CATEGORY: Food and Drink
CATEGORY: Me

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/06/beer-review-clausthaler-low-alcohol-lager.html 
DATE: 06/01/2010 11:10:59 PM
-----
BODY:
<p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834013482aafc93970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="ClausthalerLowAlcoholLagerBeer" border="0" class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834013482aafc93970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834013482aafc93970c-800wi" title="ClausthalerLowAlcoholLagerBeer" /></a><br /></p>
<p>It&#39;s been a while - sorry about that. I&#39;ve been posting very nearly every day on my other site <a href="http://www.uknatureblog.com" target="_blank">UKNatureBlog</a> so check that out for wildlife including lots from my own garden.</p><p>I&#39;ve also been busy preparing for the imminent arrival of my first child, which is exciting and already life-changing. For instance I&#39;ve now joined my wife in not drinking alcohol as I may be required to rush her off to hospital at a moment&#39;s notice. Hence I have entered the murky world of low alcohol beer! So far we&#39;ve sampled Becks Blue (zero alcohol), Cobra Zero (zero alcohol) and Clausthaler Classic (&lt; 0.5% alcohol). And I must say that Clausthaler has been a revelation that wins out over the others. It doesn&#39;t taste watery and overly bitter (like the Becks) but it&#39;s not cloying and overly malty (like the Cobra). In fact it actually tastes like a fairly normal weakish French bottled lager to me, which is a fair feat when it leaves your head clear. I know it&#39;s German but it reminds me of Kronenbourg or St Omer in stubbies when camping in France for some reason.</p><p>Actually it&#39;s truly quite wondrous stuff as you can savour a cold beer or two on a summer&#39;s afternoon and not be in the slightest bit muddled or woozy for the rest of the day. It&#39;s just refreshing, tasty and beery. I picked it up in Waitrose on a whim, but I now know that it&#39;s actually the most popular alcohol-free brand in Europe and I can see why.</p><p>I learnt a lot about it from this <a href="http://www.ciao.co.uk/Clausthaler_Bottled_Low_Alcohol_Lager__Review_5556039" target="_blank">very good and fact-filled review</a>&#0160;by a beer-craving pregnant lady, a few notables from which I&#39;ll expound upon here. Apparently they brew it in the same manner as other German purity-law beers, but with a special yeast that doesn&#39;t generate nearly so much alcohol. This sets it apart from most other low alcohol brews that pass normal strength beer through an osmosis process that knackers the flavour. Clausthaler claim that the small amount of alcohol in their beer is just enough to make it properly &#39;beery&#39; compared to those with none. Anyway - Clausthaler is thoroughly recommended, though I&#39;m still hoping to find some low-alcohol bitter in the supermarkets. The 30p a can <a href="http://www.mysupermarket.co.uk/tesco-price-comparison/Beer/Tesco_Value_Bitter_21_4x440ml.html" target="_blank">2% generic value bitter</a> you see in the low-end supermarkets doesn&#39;t count as the alcohol content is clearly just the result of penny-pinching.</p><p><br /></p>&#0160;

TITLE: Hot air balloon near miss
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg

CATEGORY: Photography

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/04/hot-air-balloon-near-miss.html 
DATE: 04/17/2010 11:27:45 PM
-----
BODY:
<p><p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340133ecc20a25970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="HotAirBalloon" class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340133ecc20a25970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340133ecc20a25970b-450wi" style="width: 450px; " /></a><br /></p></p><p>A Virgin hot air balloon slowly lumbered over our house this evening before nearly landing in the allotments behind our garden. It seemed to come within a few tens of feet of touching down before wisely thinking better of it. There are lots of greenhouses, poly-tunnels, huts, etc. not to mention a row of houses on the other side.</p><p>I don&#39;t know if the pilot was seriously considering landing there, as it seems like a really poor spot that would have ended in disaster. You might get the basket down safely if you were lucky but the balloon envelope would be a nightmare to deal with amongst the allotments. Perhaps as dusk drew in on a fairly still night the pilot was getting a bit nervous about losing light and further opportunities. Or maybe it was just for fun, buzzing low over the houses. It rose quickly with a big burst of flame and then headed off to the fields just North of St Albans where I think it must have landed.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Groovy and Grails
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: groovy-and-grails
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2010/03/groovy-and-grails.html 
DATE: 03/16/2010 04:55:23 PM
-----
BODY:
<h2>Overview</h2>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica">I&#39;ve spent the past couple of weeks reading up on <a href="http://www.grails.org/" target="_blank">Grails</a>, a web application framework inspired by Ruby on Rails. It takes advantage of <a href="http://groovy.codehaus.org/" target="_blank">Groovy</a>, a dynamic programming language that runs on the JVM and is best described as a cross between Java and Ruby. Groovy has support for most existing Java syntax, but also a lot of Ruby-style syntax and dynamic features. To be honest I find that the Java aspects of Groovy (and Grails) drag the whole experience down a bit compared to writing Ruby, but it should be significantly nicer to work with than pure Java.</p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica; min-height: 14.0px"></p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica">The big selling point of Groovy and Grails over Ruby on Rails comes from the compatibility with the whole Java ecosystem. You can have your clever dynamic language without giving up the Java libraries and deployment environments that your projects and customers may demand. This is clearly quite a compelling idea if like me you&#39;ve got a long history of doing big Java web apps but have recently been converted to the dynamic cause.</p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica; min-height: 14.0px"></p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica">One thing that is very important to realise is that Grails is not a port of Rails. It is in fact built on top of the Spring stack, including Spring MVC, REST support, dependency injection, transactionality and Hibernate amongst others. If you&#39;re already familiar with Spring this is probably a good thing, though I do worry that the framework on top of framework approach could lead to having more to learn (and mess up) overall.</p>
<h2>Practical findings</h2>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica">My initial experiments showed an interesting result that rather worried me. I set up a domain class and a controller with <em>def scaffold = true</em> to provide built-in CRUD for my domain class. However the CRUD pages this presented were noticeably sluggish in the browser and benchmarking confirmed it could only manage about 2 requests per second. This is lamentably poor and even now I can&#39;t figure out how it&#39;s managing to spend so much time achieving not very much.</p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica; min-height: 14.0px"></p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica">After a bit of experimentation I determined that if I use <em>grails generate-all</em> to put the scaffolding code in place in my classes (rather than using the dynamic scaffolding) then things speed up enormously. In fact I can now get about 200 requests per second. It&#39;s hard to see how there can be such a massive performance gulf between the two scenarios as I would have thought that the only extra overhead for dynamic scaffolding is the initial dynamic intercept of the missing action method and calling into the scaffolding code. Hopefully a Grails expert can explain this, or I will eventually figure it out myself. At least now I have determined that for real-world scenarios there isn&#39;t going to be a performance issue, though I am surprised that the Grails docs don&#39;t have a massive &quot;Warning - dynamic scaffolding is really slow&quot; box.</p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica; min-height: 14.0px"></p>
<p style="margin: 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px 0.0px; font: 12.0px Helvetica">For now I haven&#39;t done much but read the docs and try a few things. I hope to get deeper into it soon.</p>
-----

Remember if you run Grails in development mode (grails run-app) it will be slow, since it doesn't cache anything and performs file / class modification checks. To benchmark Grails create a WAR and deploy it onto your container of choice.

BODY:
<p><p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340120a7ae2856970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="MinstrelsTube" border="0" class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340120a7ae2856970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340120a7ae2856970b-800wi" title="MinstrelsTube" /></a></p></p><p>Ah Christmas (I know that&#39;s now a fading memory, humour me). An excuse to gorge to excess on rich food and drink, and an opportunity to slip extras into the supermarket trolley without the wife putting them back on the shelf! Hence a big tube of Minstrels was craftily snuck amongst the shopping and made it through the till and home. Mmmmm Minstrels.</p><p>However glee turned to disappointment when opening the tube to find that it wasn&#39;t rammed full of giant brown Smarties, but instead contained three plastic bags of Minstrels. Bah! I emptied out the bags into the tube, which was then not quite half full. A complete swizz if you ask me. They&#39;ve cunningly put them in bags so the tube doesn&#39;t rattle to give away the short-filling. I imagine I&#39;m not the only shopper who assumed the tube was full but was disappointed, and I am willing to go out on a limb and suggest that the good people at Mars/Galaxy did this cynically to con the good British public. Even if it wasn&#39;t deliberate (and I bet they&#39;ve got an &#39;official&#39; reason for using the bags) I suspect they were perfectly aware of the fact that they were selling half the amount of chocolate that the buyer would intuitively assume. Grrrr.</p>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Insulating my house
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: insulating-my-house
CATEGORY: DIY
CATEGORY: Environment

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/12/insulating-my-house.html 
DATE: 12/06/2009 10:48:38 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>Living as I do in a 1920s house with solid brick walls (no cavity, no insulation) the recent cold snap made it really clear just what a difference insulation makes. The modern flat I was in previously was bordered on most sides by other flats, and its exterior walls were no doubt well insulated. The heating didn&#39;t have to work very hard, but in the new house it had a struggle on its hands and ran nearly continuously through the freezing weather, whilst only very slowly raising the internal temperature. You could almost feel the heat (and the money, and my eco credentials) draining away through those cold solid walls.</p><p><p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834012876209ae7970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="SlabInsulation" border="0" class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834012876209ae7970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834012876209ae7970c-800wi" title="SlabInsulation" /></a></p></p><p>I&#39;ve been on a mission to improve the odds in my favour since I moved in, having done the following.</p><p><ul>
<li>Improve sealing around the front door, which was quite draughty, by adding self-adhesive strips of V-profile sealant tape. Did the same for a draughty window.</li>
<li>Improve sealing around the back UPVC doors by adding shims to the closing mechanism&#0160;and adjusting the locking system&#0160;to get it to close more tightly and cut out the draught.</li>
<li>Put some clear tape (literally just ordinary Sellotape) on the frame of a UPVC window that was allowing a tiny draught. This wasn&#39;t the gap between window and frame but an imperceptible gap in the frame itself!</li>
<li>Installed secondary double glazing on the few single glazed windows in the house. This is a clear film that you stick up on the inside with double sided tape then shrink smooth with a hairdryer. The air gap that you trap acts just the same as normal double glazing and is not immediately noticeable.</li>
<li>Improved loft insulation by adding 150mm <a href="http://www.space-insulation.com/blanket.html" target="_blank">space blankets</a> in most of the eaves. These are great - easy to handle because they&#39;re wrapped in thin plastic and crazy cheap at most hardware stores because they&#39;re heavily subsidised. I had to be careful not to lay them over wires though (they might overheat) which is tricky as most of the rooms below have a light in the middle of them and hence wires going to those lights from the loft. I&#39;d love to know how everyone else solves this wiry problem, given pretty much everyone must have it.</li>
<li>Added 50mm slab insulation (glass fibre insulation bought in flat semi-rigid slabs, rather than in rolls) to the backs of the plasterboard walls in the converted loft. This stuff is great as you just cut it with a knife to slightly larger than the size required and jam it into the spaces, where it stays. I got a single pack from <a href="http://www.wickes.co.uk/General-Purpose-Insulation-Slab/invt/161197" target="_blank">Wickes</a>, which covers over 7 square metres and only cost me £15 because the pack was damaged and they gave me a discount. I reckon that&#39;s £15 well spent and I hope it will make the loft noticeably warmer. The pictures show this stuff being cut (above) and installed (below) with the left-most bit of wall still to do, showing just the shiny back of the plasterboard - so you can see before and after in one shot.</li>
</ul>
<p style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340120a71e3e0d970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><span style="color: #000000; -webkit-text-decorations-in-effect: none; "><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340120a71e3e0d970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="SlabInsulation2" border="0" class="asset asset-image at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340120a71e3e0d970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340120a71e3e0d970b-800wi" title="SlabInsulation2" /></a></span><br /></a></p></p>

COMMENT:
