AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Beer Review: Lindemans Framboise
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: beer-review-lindemans-framboise
CATEGORY: Beer
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/06/beer-review-lindemans-framboise.html 
DATE: 06/19/2009 08:25:33 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>This is a decidedly exotic beer. In fact you may struggle to identify it as beer at all if presented a glass of it. To look at, it&#39;s a deep, ever so slightly cloudy colour that can only really be described as &#39;raspberry&#39;. Which isn&#39;t surprising because that&#39;s what&#39;s in it - 30% of it in fact.</div><br /><div>It tastes like finely sparkling raspberry juice with a slightly tart/sour beery edge. Those non-fruit flavours are the Belgian Lambic beer - fermented with yeasts that simply drift in on the air and give Lambics their characteristic sourness - just like sourdough bread in principle. Some Lambics can be face-puckeringly sour, but this has so much sweet fruit that it&#39;s tempered to just a cheeky edge that&#39;s enough to make it interesting. At only 2.5% alcohol by volume and with so much fruit, it&#39;s got to be almost good for you! Ones probably enough at one sitting though, before moving onto something a bit more normal.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115712ff889970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="LindemansFramboise 7214" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340115712ff889970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115712ff889970b-350wi" style="width: 350px; " /></a> <br /></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Setting an app path prefix in Ramaze
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: setting-an-app-path-prefix-in-ramaze-with-globalprefix
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/06/setting-an-app-path-prefix-in-ramaze-with-globalprefix.html 
DATE: 06/17/2009 07:13:57 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>Say you&#39;ve got a Ramaze web application quite happily running at http://foo.com/ but you want to have the whole app running at http://foo.com/myapp/. It&#39;s not unusual to want to do this, for various reasons, and it&#39;s relatively easy to arrange by setting a single piece of Ramaze config.</p>

<p>The exact way to do this depends on the version of Ramaze you&#39;re using, as the options system changed between the 2009.03 and 2009.04 gem versions. Here&#39;s the syntax for Ramaze version 2009.03 and previous:</p>

<p><span style="font-family: Courier;">Ramaze::Global.prefix = &#39;/myapp&#39;</span></p>

<p><span style="font-family: &#39;Trebuchet MS&#39;;">And here&#39;s the syntax for Ramaze 2009.04 and later, which requires setting the prefix for links (as before) and a routing rule for stripping it off incoming requests (since it no longer does it automatically):</span></p>

<p><span style="font-family: Courier, Verdana, sans-serif;">app_prefix = &#39;/myapp&#39;</span></p>

<p><span style="font-family: Courier, Verdana, sans-serif;">Ramaze.options.prefix = app_prefix</span></p>

<p><span style="font-family: Courier, Verdana, sans-serif;">Ramaze::Route[ /^#{app_prefix}(.*)$/ ] = &#39;%s&#39;</span></p>

<p>The prefix will be automatically added onto the front of all URLs created by Ramaze&#39;s built-in URL and link creator methods (R, Rs and A from the link helper). It will also be stripped off the front of all incoming requests by the dispatcher (for Ramaze 2009.03) or by the routing rule (for later versions). So your own application code doesn&#39;t need to be aware that it&#39;s running with a prefix at all, unless you create absolute URLs via means other than the link helper methods. In that case you will need to use Ramaze::Global.prefix or Ramaze.options.prefix in your own link construction code to get them right.</p>

<p>One thing which might catch you out is forgetting to put the forward slash on the front of the prefix. If you set the prefix to &#39;myapp&#39; it won&#39;t work - it has to be &#39;/myapp&#39;.</p>

<p></p>

<p><strong>Update:</strong> Note that static files (those in your public directory) are not affected by any of the above changes. Furthermore, chances are that you refer to them with absolute paths from your HTML anyway - e.g. for images, CSS, JavaScript. So further thought will be required to handle these.</p>
-----

DATE: 06/16/2009 01:15:09 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Apple have finally patched up some serious security holes in their Java distributions with Mac OS X. I had a problem when trying to install it, getting the simple message <em>The update &quot;Java for OS X 10.5 Update 4&quot; can&#39;t be installed</em> almost immediately after pressing the Install button (it didn&#39;t even start downloading). I&#39;d just freshly started up my Mac and the browser wasn&#39;t running (it specifically instructs you to quit all browsers) so that wasn&#39;t the problem.</div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115711d0315970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="JavaUpdate4Fail" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340115711d0315970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115711d0315970b-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a>&#0160;</div><div>Repairing permissions on my startup disk with Disk Utility then restarting seemed to fix it and allow the installation to proceed. AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Snow Leopard Gamma 2.2
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: snow-leopard-gamma-22
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/06/snow-leopard-gamma-22.html 
DATE: 06/08/2009 09:37:52 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>WWDC has begun and more information is trickling out about Mac OS X 10.6 &quot;Snow Leopard&quot;. I like what I see, but one thing really stood out as intriguing: default gamma will be 2.2 rather than the traditional Mac setting of 1.8. It&#39;s one of the many items mentioned on this page:&#0160;<a href="http://www.apple.com/macosx/refinements/enhancements-refinements.html" target="_blank">http://www.apple.com/macosx/refinements/enhancements-refinements.html</a>. This is going to be important for photographers and graphic artists in particular.</div><br /><div>What does it mean? Gamma refers to the way numerical colour representations are mapped to real colours on the screen - specifically how the luminance is mapped, i.e. how light they appear. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma_correction" target="_blank">Wikipedia explains gamma in full</a>, complete with some neat sample images to show how different gamma settings affect the way an image looks. Windows PCs traditionally use system gamma setting of 2.2 whereas Macs go for 1.8, which means that the same image tends to look darker on the PC than on the Mac, assuming that the image doesn&#39;t contain colour profile information, which is the case for most images and videos on the web. Mac users create pictures to look just right on their monitor and are disappointed when the image looks dark and dingy on their PC using friends&#39; machines. Conversely, the PC artist is surprised that the image they create looks bright and washed out on their friends&#39; Macs. And neither of them generally have a clue that this is because of the different gamma settings of the systems!</div><br /><div>It seems that Apple have decided to bite the bullet and get in line with the popular 2.2 standard to end this perennial confusion. This is a major step for Apple to take and I imagine they had to think long and hard over it, but it&#39;s probably for the best in the long term.</div>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: iPhone Dev: GPS vs Cell Triangulation vs WiFi
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: iphone-dev-gps-vs-cell-triangulation-vs-wifi
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/06/iphone-dev-gps-vs-cell-triangulation-vs-wifi.html 
DATE: 06/04/2009 11:38:49 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;ve been working on an iPhone app that uses location data extensively. From poring through the raw data that comes from CLLocationManager I&#39;ve learned a few interesting things. In particular it seems that CLLocation objects with negative (i.e. invalid) verticalAccuracy value were likely derived from non-GPS sources - e.g. triangulation from cell towers or SkyHook WiFi positioning. In my app I really need only the purest, most accurate data so I&#39;m choosing to ignore non-GPS data and this is how I can tell it apart in order to do that.</div><br /><div>It took a while for this realisation to dawn on me as I was seeing occasional poor data points mixed in with the good ones, and this seems to be because it occasionally slips in a non-GPS location in certain circumstances.</div>
-----
EXTENDED BODY:

-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: iPhone app debugging: stop on exception
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: iphone-app-debugging-stop-on-exception
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/06/iphone-app-debugging-stop-on-exception.html 
DATE: 06/02/2009 10:36:09 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Objective-C can be a difficult mistress at the best of times, but I&#39;ve been pulling my hair out over the useless exception traces it gives when my iPhone app falls over. For example:</div><br /><div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">2009-06-02 22:22:39.036 GPSDraw[31024:20b] *** -[CLLocation timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate]: unrecognized selector sent to instance 0x40add0</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">2009-06-02 22:22:39.038 GPSDraw[31024:20b] *** Terminating app due to uncaught exception &#39;NSInvalidArgumentException&#39;, reason: &#39;*** -[CLLocation timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate]: unrecognized selector sent to instance 0x40add0&#39;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">2009-06-02 22:22:39.039 GPSDraw[31024:20b] Stack: (</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2532991147,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2419396155,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2533020330,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2533013676,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2533013874,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2532962733,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;23499,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;22944,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2520474771,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2532494021,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;2532494456,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;827745792,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;827745989,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;816114848,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;816160924,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;11116,</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;10970</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">)</span></div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Which is all very exciting, but how about telling me the line of my code that threw the exception?</span> That&#39;s what any sane IDE would do, but XCode is different and prefers to give an unhelpful list of numbers instead. The stack that the debugger shows is always just some arbitrary point in the main run loop, with the only bit of my code in scope being main.</div><br /><div>However I&#39;ve <a href="http://www.cocoadev.com/index.pl?DebuggingTechniques" target="_blank">stumbled across</a> the crucial way to get the behaviour that should be enabled by default. Whilst debugging, select Run &gt; Manage Breakpoints &gt; Add Symbolic Breakpoint. Now enter &quot;<span style="font-family: Helvetica; line-height: 16px; ">objc_exception_throw&quot; without the quotes and it should actually break at the point in your code that the exception is thrown.</span></div></div>

<div>If you go to Pizza Express, this is what you must order to drink! They may only serve two different beers (the other being bog standard Peroni) but it's worth it just for this one. <div>This is a challenging beer. It sticks two fingers up to you, even from the shelf with it&#39;s &quot;I&#39;m not a fuddy duddy ale&quot; packaging. AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: XCode Build Target Switching - Gets Me Every Time!
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: xcode-build-target-switching-gets-me-every-time
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/xcode-build-target-switching-gets-me-every-time.html 
DATE: 05/26/2009 11:12:36 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;m working on an iPhone app right now, which mostly involves dev/test against the simulator, with occasional runs on a real iPhone to check everything is running the same there. But every time I flip that menu in the XCode toolbar from Simulator to Device and hit cmd-R I spend five minutes of confusion trying to figure out things aren&#39;t working even nearly right.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570a82ddb970b-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Picture 2" border="0" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570a82ddb970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570a82ddb970b-800wi" title="Picture 2" /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">After a while and maybe a bit of debugging I&#39;ll get a hunch that all is not as it seems, I&#39;ll do a complete clean and rebuild and then my code will behave perfectly, with XCode whistling&#0160;cherubically&#0160;with an innocent look on its face. All that remains is for me to check the Build menu to reassure myself that cmd-R does in fact do &quot;<span style="font-style: italic;">Build</span> and Run&quot; which it most certainly does.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">I can only assume that there is a bug in XCode that doesn&#39;t correctly reset build state when switching between targets, so it thinks my build is up to date when in fact it&#39;s ancient.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Browser Zooming
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: browser-zooming
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/browser-zooming.html 
DATE: 05/20/2009 08:59:46 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;ve done a lot of web development in my time and a lot of that time is usually sucked up in trying to make a site &#39;scale&#39; well &#0160;- not in terms of dealing with vast quantities of users, but responding to changes in font size without the page layout going completely screwy.</div><br /><div>Generally the user can change the font size on the fly to suit themselves, and as a page designer you really ought to cater to their whim. This means that you can&#39;t assume that your carefully setup sidebar links will always require just one line each, or that your box titles will always fit in the space available. It can be a nightmare to get right and if you have a very &#39;designed&#39; page you often have to accept that it will only look reasonable within a certain font size range. Here&#39;s one of my blog posts unzoomed, looking handsome as always:</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115709a4ff4970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrowserZoom1" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340115709a4ff4970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115709a4ff4970b-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a> <br /></div><div>I&#39;ve noticed that web browsers have increasingly been looking to solve this problem without troubling the poor page designers. Safari 4 Beta and Firefox 3 now default to simply scaling the entire page rather than just the text, though you can select a &quot;Zoom Text Only&quot; menu option to go back to the previous behaviour. By zooming every last pixel of the page identically, page layout and design are kept together and in the originally intended proportions no matter what, though any images on the page may start to look a bit fuzzy as they get zoomed up beyond their native size.</div><br /><div>Here&#39;s that same blog post of mine with text-only-zoom and then everything-zoom in Safari:</div><br /><div><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156fa50277970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrowserZoom2" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156fa50277970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156fa50277970c-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a>&#0160;<a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156fa502a5970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BrowserZoom3" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156fa502a5970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156fa502a5970c-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a> <br /></div><div>You&#39;ll notice that Typepad have done a really good job of making their site text-zoom friendly, and with everything zoomed (and not zoomed quite so much) the whole page is too wide to be usable. Horses for courses I suppose - there are going to be cases when each method is superior and hopefully users (ones that care about zooming at all) will be savvy enough to choose what works for them.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: That Syncing Feeling
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: that-syncing-feeling
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/that-syncing-feeling.html 
DATE: 05/16/2009 09:44:45 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I sync my calendars across two Macs (desktop and laptop) using the horrifically named MobileMe. It mostly works fine (apart from the fact it often <a href="/atc/2009/04/mobileme-sync-problems.html">fails to sync some entries</a> - a minor thing) but there is one irritation that&#39;s really starting to bug me.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f978856970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Picture 1" border="0" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f978856970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f978856970c-800wi" title="Picture 1" /></a>&#0160;</div><div>I get up in the morning and I wake up my desktop to read email and news over my Coco Pops, whereupon I often get some iCal reminders for the day - which I usually snooze to re-remind me later. Then at some later point I pick up my laptop and immediately after opening it up I get the same reminders (fair enough) and snooze them too. A minute later, after my laptop has synced I get a popup informing me of the clash of snoozed times, which I have to resolve and re-sync. I play this game all day, with sync conflicts ping-ponging between the two machines. It&#39;s completely ridiculous.</div><div style="text-align: center;"></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115708d921b970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Picture 7" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340115708d921b970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115708d921b970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a></div><div> What would I prefer? Well I can understand the impeccable logic it&#39;s applying - it&#39;s just unfortunate the timing of events that leads to this result. How about the system always assuming that the last &#39;command&#39; I gave is definitive and should automatically be applied without asking me on the other machines? Maybe I should email Steve, er Phil, and suggest it.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Objective-C instance variable naming and use
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: objectivec-instance-variable-naming-and-use
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/objectivec-instance-variable-naming-and-use.html 
DATE: 05/15/2009 08:28:33 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;ve been writing some iPhone apps recently and have thus been re-exposed to the horror that is Objective-C. I&#39;ve written plenty of Objective-C over the years, but coming back to it from proper modern languages like Java, C# and Ruby is just painful. It&#39;s really showing it&#39;s age and I harbour a hope that the macruby work Apple&#39;s doing right now is actually going to become the official way to write Mac and even iPhone software over the coming couple of years. That would be just perfect as the underlying Objective-C stuff would still be there for compatibility&#39;s sake and performance where necessary. Go on Apple, you know you want to!</div><br /><div>Anyway, that mini-rant was not what I came here for. I&#39;ve been wrestling with the best way to name and use instance variables. Version 1 was to name them in no special way at all, but then you end up with confusion between properties and the underlying variable and also with methods that want to use a parameter with the same sensible name (as is often the case with init methods):</div><br /><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">@interface Person : NSObject</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">{</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;NSString *name;</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">}</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">@property(nonatomic, retain) NSString *name;</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">@end</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; "><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">@implementation Person</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">@synthesize name;</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">// This gives a compiler warning because parameter name masks the instance var.</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">// Calling the parameter anything else is ridiculous though.</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">- (id)initWithName:(NSString *)name</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">{</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;...</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;// Need to remember to use self.name rather than name if we want to use the property.</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;// It&#39;s easy to accidentally just assign directly to name and get a messed up retain count.</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;self.name = name;</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;....</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">}</span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; font-family: Courier; ">@end</span></div><br /><div>So I&#39;ve decided it&#39;s probably superior to use a prefix for the instance var itself, and though I&#39;d always used m in the past, I&#39;m going for _ here because it seems to be an Objective-C convention:</div><br /><div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">@interface Person : NSObject</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">{</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;NSString *_name;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">}</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">@property(nonatomic, retain) NSString *name;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">@end</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">@implementation Person</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">// Note that we have to use some extra syntax here to hook up the non-underscored property.</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">@synthesize name = _name;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">// Now the param doesn&#39;t clash with anything.</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">- (id)initWithName:(NSString *)name</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">{</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;...</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;// Property works exactly as before, but direct assignment with _name = name really</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;// stands out so we&#39;re only likely&#0160;to do it knowingly.</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;self.name = name;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">}</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">@end</span></span></div><br /><div>Finally I end up agonising over whether I should always access my instance variables via a property (self.whatever), or whether I&#39;m OK to use them directly (_whatever). Some purists argue that it&#39;s worth encapsulating with a property and always using that property. This way you can change your underlying data types but potentially retain the same property methods without breaking the code that uses them - i.e. it&#39;s an extra layer of abstraction that protects you from breaking changes. A few years ago I might have bought that, but these days I only add in layers of abstraction when they&#39;re truly needed, so I will use the property for external access and the instance var directly for internal access (though I&#39;ll always assign to the property to ensure retain counts are correct). I can easily refactor to add in the abstraction if necessary at a later point, but if I don&#39;t need it now then I won&#39;t bother with it.</div></div>

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Tony Arnold
EMAIL: tonyarnold@me.com
IP: 220.233.229.247
URL: http://tonyarnold.com
DATE: 06/05/2009 03:25:01 PM
I tend to prefix my instance variables with 'm_', or just 'm' so I end up with 'm_Name' (I'm not a fan of underscores, but they're very clear). This makes it nice and easy to see what's a member of the class, and what's local to the method I'm working in.

You should always access your instance variables via setters, getters or properties - only in init and dealloc should you do anything with your instance variables directly. My advice with properties and the dot syntax is that if you're going to use it, stick with it for everything - don't alternate between standard [self setBlah:...]; and self.blah = ...;
-----
COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Dean Nevins
EMAIL: nevins@sbcc.edu
IP: 68.6.99.157
URL: 
DATE: 09/07/2009 07:07:12 AM
You shouldn't use "_" to prefix your instance variables since those are reserved for use by Apple. See: <a href="http://developer.apple.com/mac/library/documentation/Cocoa/Conceptual/ObjectiveC/Articles/ocLanguageSummary.html">http://developer.apple.com/mac/library/documentation/Cocoa/Conceptual/ObjectiveC/Articles/ocLanguageSummary.html</a>
-----
COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Sam3
EMAIL: 
IP: 86.133.255.141
URL: http://profile.typepad.com/1220903235s28378
DATE: 09/07/2009 07:10:53 AM
Actually it's just method names for which Apple reserves underscore prefixing. In my example, the instance variable has an underscore, but the synthesised accessor method does not, so this is OK.

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Al
EMAIL: macness@me.com
IP: 209.202.75.15
URL: 
DATE: 12/03/2010 06:04:52 PM
Myself, I don't write stuff like this:

- (id)initWithName:(NSString *)name

I use this instead:

- (id)initWithName:(NSString *)newName

avoids the compiler issue, saves a lot of (unnecessary) awkward _'s

I agree with Tony Arnold, only use instance variables directly in init or dealloc's.  The reason do it directly in init methods, is that at the point of initialization, sometimes an accessor method will have some kind of dependance on another instance variable that is not yet initialized (ex; for parameter checking, validation etc).  So you need to get the object set up without having to run into those dependancies.  Using instance variables directly elsewhere can cause you to inadvertently bypass internal logic though, so you don't want to do that outside of init/dealloc

C# has nowhere near the elegance of obj-c imho.  Invocations, Message Forwarding, KVO, Notifications etc. are extremely powerful features.  I don't see that in C#/.NET. Not to mention all the insane overloads...good grief.  Talk about clunky.  And the .NET frameworks look like a major ripoff of Cocoa. (The only horror I see in obj-c is the dot syntax!)

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: New Ramaze
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: new-ramaze
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/new-ramaze.html 
DATE: 05/11/2009 07:00:10 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>My favourite Ruby web application framework&#0160;<a href="http://ramaze.net/" target="_blank">Ramaze</a>&#0160;has been updated, and in a major way. As of the 2009.05 gem release Ramaze is built on top of sub-project Innate - a lean mean core that in turn builds on top of Rack. Pistos explains the major changes well in <a href="http://blog.purepistos.net/index.php/2009/05/06/ramaze-200905-released/" target="_blank">his blog post</a>. For me, although I&#39;m yet to properly get to grips with the new code, the most promising things to look forward to are:</div><div><ul>
<li>A fresh start with a lot of accumulated cruft thrown out, resulting in cleaner, more straightforward code for Ramaze itself. I end up reading the Ramaze code a lot (it&#39;s very accessible and empowering) so this makes it even easier to delve into the internals and figure out what&#39;s what.</li>
<li><span>Rack middleware is front and centre, making it easy to chain HTTP processing components into your request handling flow - like Java servlet filters.</span>&#0160;Not required very often, but a godsend when it is.</li>
<li><span>A much tidier sessions implementation, with lazy&#0160;</span>initialisation.</li>
<li>A rethought render helper that brings a bit more sanity to the many ways to bring in view fragments.</li>
<li>Layout support is more of a core concern, with more facilities to deal with layouts sensibly.</li>
<li><span>Built-in support for serving different content representations</span>&#0160;from the same controller.</li>
<li><span>An interesting new codebase for me to poke my nose into :-)</span>&#0160;</li>
</ul>
<span>Now if only I can find time to get my Ramaze CRUD framework going in this new world order, then package it up to share with the rest of the world. One day!</span><br /></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Install Ramaze and Innate on Mac OS X's stock Ruby
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: install-ramaze-and-innate-on-mac-os-xs-stock-ruby
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/install-ramaze-and-innate-on-mac-os-xs-stock-ruby.html 
DATE: 05/05/2009 10:21:14 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Mac OS X comes with a lot of fantastic stuff built-in, like Java, Ruby, Python etc. The downside to this is that users tend to stick with the stock installs of those items and never upgrade them, which can leave them a long way behind the curve. I just ran into that problem when trying to install the latest version of <a href="http://ramaze.net" target="_blank">Ramaze</a> (my favourite Ruby web application framework) from the official gems. The IRC channel #ramaze was useful as always and provided the solution. The problem is that rubygems on OSX 10.5 is old and tired, but you can tell it to install a very specific version of innate that sorts it out. So from scratch, assuming Mac OS X 10.5 with the stock Ruby 1.8.6 install:</div><div><br /><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt; sudo gem install innate -v 2009.04<br /></span><span><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt; sudo gem install ramaze</span></span><br /></div><br /><div>Obviously as new versions of innate and ramaze come out, the exact version of innate required will change. You can tell which verison you need by trying the ramaze install and looking at the error message.</div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update:</span> Actually now Ramaze and Innate 2009.05 have been released, it seems things work just fine without any special incantations. So you can just <span style="font-family: Courier;">sudo gem install ramaze</span> and you&#39;re away.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Attingham Park
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: attingham-park
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Travel

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/05/attingham-park.html 
DATE: 05/04/2009 07:16:45 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>On the way back from a lovely wedding in Shropshire over the bank holiday weekend, we stopped off at <a href="http://www.nationaltrust.org.uk/main/w-attinghampark" target="_blank">Attingham Park</a> - a National Trust property comprising a big house and a bigger estate. We got there fairly early in the day, and since the house isn&#39;t open to the massed hordes until 1pm we snuck on a free tour at 11. This turned out to be an unexpected delight, as the tour was specifically about the usually hidden process of keeping a big old NT house clean throughout the seasons. I didn&#39;t realise quite how much careful effort goes in every day just to keep dust at bay, not to mention light, insects and humidity.</div><br /><div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769a58970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="AttinghamPillars 6606" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f769a58970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769a58970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>&#0160;<br /></div><br /><div>To me the house isn&#39;t the star attraction here though (we went back and had a full look once it was properly open). The vast parkland grounds are what make it special, featuring grassland, woodland, deer, rivers and ponds. These are lightly littered with some interesting sculptures, which would be more impressive if they weren&#39;t accompanied by ridiculous texts explaining what they represent. I should have burned that leaflet! We almost missed the monopoly hotels on sticks poking out of the water of a pond.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769be4970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="AttinghamMonopolyHotels 6609" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f769be4970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769be4970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><br /><div>As we strolled through the bracken there was a section that was literally swarming with large very black and rather lugubrious flies. I&#39;ve since identified these as St Mark&#39;s Flies, which apparently emerge at this time of year - traditionally 25th April (hence the name).</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769ce4970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="StMarksFly 6608" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f769ce4970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769ce4970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><br /><div>Down by the river (I&#39;m not sure if it was the Severn or the Tern) there were a couple of Grey Wagtails shooting out over the water for insects.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769d70970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="GreyWagtail 6607" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f769d70970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f769d70970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Chicago Town "The Deep Dish" Pizza
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: chicago-town-the-deep-dish-pizza
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/chicago-town-the-deep-dish-pizza.html 
DATE: 04/30/2009 09:19:17 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Stuck for something for dinner and late home from work this evening I picked up a complete bargain at Iceland: £1 for a pack of two Chicago Town &quot;The Deep Dish&quot; pepperoni pizzas. I&#39;ve always liked these and at this price you can&#39;t go wrong.</div><br /><div>I liked these particular instances even if I slightly overcooked them, but my enjoyment was tempered by the arguably false advertising on the box, which boasts about how the pizza is<span style="font-style: italic;"> &quot;</span><span style="font-weight: bold;"><span style="font-style: italic;">topped to overflowing</span></span><span style="font-style: italic;"> with tomato sauce,&#0160;pepperoni&#0160;slices and so much melt-in-the-mouth mozzarella cheese, that </span><span style="font-weight: bold;"><span style="font-style: italic;">it takes a deep dish base to hold all the topping in</span></span><span style="font-style: italic;">&quot;</span>. The emphasis is theirs. They even illustrate the point with a photo demonstrating the approximately 15:85 base:topping ratio, complete with oodles of that tempting cheese. Here&#39;s their photo on the left, and mine on the right.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f6b1bca970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="DeepDishPizzaTheirs 6565" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f6b1bca970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f6b1bca970c-350wi" style="width: 350px; " /></a> <a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570614445970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="DeepDishPizzaMine 6564" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570614445970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570614445970b-350wi" style="width: 350px; " /></a>&#0160;<br /></div><br /><div>Now I&#39;m no investigative journalist but it seems like the 15:85 ratio has been reversed for the actual pizza on my plate compared to the picture on the box. And the near centimetre deep layer of mozzarella? Missing in action. Don&#39;t get me wrong - still a bargain for £1 and damn tasty with it - but they&#39;ve stuck their neck on the block here and should reap the scorn deserved.</div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update:</span> I sent an email to the customer service department who have responded to ensure me that the batch in question did not record any anomalies, so my disappointingly topped pizza must have been a one-off. That must go for all four pizzas in the two boxes I bought! They&#39;ve sent me a voucher for a free pizza to ease the pain. Fair enough, but this is clearly a stock response and I suspect they know full well it wasn&#39;t a one off and that their pizzas are always a long way from meeting the promise on the box. Cynical, moi? Can I be bothered to continue to kick up a fuss? I don&#39;t think that big corporations should be able to get away with this sort of blatant deceit and disrespect for the customer, but it&#39;s just not worth it.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Apache Bench Notes
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: apache-bench-notes
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/apache-bench-notes.html 
DATE: 04/30/2009 07:16:56 AM
-----
BODY:
<div>Many people don&#39;t know about Apache Bench, even though it may be installed on their system. For instance it&#39;s installed on Mac OS X by default. It&#39;s a very simple command line utility to perform basic stress/performance testing of websites. It can request a given URL repeatedly, then report back statistics. I generally use it in this form:</div><br /><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt; ab -n 100 -c 4 http://www.somewebsite.com/test/page.htm</span></div><br /><div>Dissecting that command:</div><div><ul>
<li>ab is the Apache Bench executable itself (found in /usr/sbin on my machine)</li>
<li><span>-n 100 tells it to send the request 100 times</span>&#0160;</li>
<li><span>-c 4 tells it to use 4 concurrent threads to do so</span></li>
<li><span>finally we have the URL itself.</span>&#0160;</li>
</ul>
Even though it&#39;s simple, there are some important gotchas worth knowing about:</div><div><ul>
<li>Remember to put the URL in single quotes if it contains characters that would otherwise be interpreted by the shell. This is often the case for any URL with a query string, as the &amp; characters will mess things up otherwise.&#0160;</li>
<li><span>If using a base URL for a site (just host name), you must use a trailing slash. http://foo.com doesn&#39;t work, but http://foo.com/ does work.</span>&#0160;</li>
<li><span>Quite often you may see in the statistics &quot;Failed requests: 5&quot; or similar, followed by a list of the types of failure: &quot;(Connect: 0, Receive: 0, Length: 5, Exceptions: 0)&quot;. If the only type of failure that actually occurred is &#39;Length&#39; then don&#39;t be alarmed. This simply means that each request (for the same URL) returned a different length response, which ab regards as suspicious. However it&#39;s perfectly normal for dynamic webpages, especially if they include the time or other very dynamic data on the page.</span>&#0160;</li>
<li><span>I find on one of my machines that it just doesn&#39;t like localhost as a hostname, so I have to use 127.0.0.1 instead. I have no idea why, as localhost works fine in other contexts on the same machine.</span>&#0160;</li>
</ul>
</div>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: 1973
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: 1973
CATEGORY: Culture
CATEGORY: Music

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/1973.html 
DATE: 04/28/2009 10:01:56 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Over the last ten years or so I&#39;ve increasingly been drawn to conclude that 1973 was a cracking year for music, and much underrated by the establishment. Here&#39;s why (and of course many of these artists released whole albums full of their best works):</div><div><ul>
<li>David Bowie - Life on Mars</li>
<li>Elton John - Crocodile Rock</li>
<li>Roberta Flack -&#0160;Killing Me Softly With His Song</li>
<li>Carly Simon -&#0160;You&#39;re So Vain</li>
<li>Bobby &quot;Boris&quot; Pickett - Monster Mash&#0160;</li>
<li><span>Wings - Live and Let Die</span>&#0160;</li>
<li><span>Stevie Wonder - Superstition</span></li>
<li>Lou Reed -&#0160;Walk On The Wild Side</li>
<li><span>Paul Simon - Kodachrome</span>&#0160;</li>
<li>Eric Weissburg &amp; Steve Mandell&#0160;- Duelling Banjos</li>
<li><span>Doobie Brothers - Long Train Running</span>&#0160;</li>
<li>Stealers Wheel - Stuck In The Middle With You</li>
<li>Money - Pink Floyd</li>
<li><span>Alice Cooper - No More Mr Nice Guy</span>&#0160;</li>
<li><span>The Who -&#0160;</span>Quadrophenia</li>
</ul>
<span>It seems that I&#39;m not the only one to think this mind you, with plenty of posts in forums out there arguing the same point. 1977 and 1971 are also very popular years from the 1970s. I should point out that I wasn&#39;t even born in those years, so I&#39;m not simply harking back to my formative years!</span>&#0160;<br /><ul>
</ul>
</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Basil Faulty
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: basil-faulty
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Science

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/basil-faulty.html 
DATE: 04/22/2009 10:56:29 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;m getting better at keeping those supermarket pots of fresh herbs alive and well on the kitchen windowsill. The trick is to repot them into a larger pot with some half-decent compost. However I always seem to be undone by aphid infestations. I have a lovely bushy basil plant right now but it&#39;s literally crawling with aphids so I suppose it&#39;ll have to go in the bin. Either that or I accept that the basil on my pasta will be contributing protein as well as leafy goodness.</div><br /><div>At least it makes an interesting subject for some macro photography! So it&#39;s out with the Sigma 105mm macro lens and my tripod and away we go. I have a couple of tips to impart, with photographic evidence to back them up.</div><br /><div>When you&#39;re doing macro work the tiniest movement can ruin the photo. And I do mean the tiniest amount of movement. On the left is a photo taken by simply pressing the shutter button, which has come out disappointingly blurry, whereas on the right I&#39;ve used both a 5 second self-timer and a delayed exposure: a feature of the Nikon D300 where the mirror goes up then it waits another second or so before actually taking the picture. Note that both these shots are taken with a sturdy tripod and the same exposure settings (1/10s, f11).</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f4a27e6970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="AphidMacroComparison" border="0" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f4a27e6970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f4a27e6970c-800wi" title="AphidMacroComparison" /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">The picture on the right is clearly sharper. This is because the vibrations from my hand pressing the shutter button and from the reflex mirror flipping out of the way have had time to die down before the picture was taken. This is a crop at 50%, so there&#39;s even more detail to be had at 100%. A good way to appreciate the ever-present vibrations, even when working on a tripod, is to turn on Live View on the camera and zoom right in to 100%. You can see the jitter due to vibration right on the LCD screen of your DSLR, and just how much it is affected by simply touching the camera or tripod.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Here&#39;s another shot, with an evil looking beastie in the middle and a cute little one at the bottom.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157040ae8f970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BasilAphids 6552" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157040ae8f970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157040ae8f970b-450wi" style="width: 450px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">[Incidentally, this is clearly the best title for a blog post I&#39;ve ever come up with.]</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Rye Meads Butterflies
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: rye-meads-butterflies
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/rye-meads-butterflies.html 
DATE: 04/22/2009 09:20:03 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Finally, to finish off my posts on Rye Meads nature reserve, the butterflies. It was a beautiful spring day and there were a fair few about, mostly the species shown below. I took all of these photos with an 80-400mm zoom lens at the 400mm end which can only focus about 6.5 feet away as a minimum. I think they&#39;ve come out remarkably well, and this approach has the advantage of not having to get really close to the butterfly, scaring it off as you might with a macro lens. Maybe I&#39;ve stumbled into a brilliant new butterfly photography technique! Remember that as always you can click on any photo for a larger version.<div><br /><div>This is a Comma butterfly, notable for the shapely edge to its wing. It&#39;s known as a Comma because of the small comma shaped mark on the underside of the wing, but you&#39;ll rarely see that.<br /><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570404596970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadButteflyComma 6485" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570404596970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570404596970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Next, an Orange-tip butterfly on a Dandelion. The name is fairly self-explanatory. For the plant as well frankly.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157040467c970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadButteflyOrangeTip 6486" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157040467c970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157040467c970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">And finally a Peacock butterfly, with the classic eyes on the wings to scare off predators. I somehow overcame my terror and got close enough to photograph it with as much detail as a 400mm lens at 6.5 feet will allow!</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570404862970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadButteflyPeacock 6487" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570404862970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570404862970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div></div></div></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Rye Meads - part III
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: rye-meads-part-iii
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/rye-meads-part-iii.html 
DATE: 04/17/2009 07:38:28 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>See&#0160;<a href="/atc/2009/04/rye-meads-rspb-reserve.html" style="color: blue; text-decoration: underline; cursor: pointer; " target="_blank">my first</a> and <a href="/atc/2009/04/more-from-rye-meads-rspb-reserve.html" target="_blank">second</a> Rye Meads posts to get up to date with the action so far. It&#39;s thrilling, honestly.</div><br /><div>This is the last set of birds, with butterflies still to come. A bumper crop today, starting with a female Pochard (at the top) and more excitingly, a Lapwing. It&#39;s not especially rare, though it was the only one I saw at Rye Meads, but it&#39;s clearly quite an exotic looking winged beast and I hadn&#39;t seen one before. Apologies for the poor photo - it was a long way away,</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157026b5fb970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadLapwing 6481" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157026b5fb970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157026b5fb970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><br /><div>Next a pair of Gadwalls - male on the left, female on the right. It&#39;s really very similar to a Mallard in most respects, especially the female which is most easily recognised as a Gadwall by the nearby presence of the more obvious male! And it lacks the blue/green flash on the wing of a Mallard.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157026c0a9970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadGadwallMale 6482" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157026c0a9970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157026c0a9970b-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a>&#0160;&#0160;<a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f3010b9970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadGadwallFemale 6483" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f3010b9970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f3010b9970c-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Here&#39;s a pair of Cormorants on a perch on a raft. If only I had something further to say about Cormorants.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157026c5ac970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadCormorants 6484" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157026c5ac970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157026c5ac970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Finally, what I believe to be a Kestrel - one of a pair that were soaring high above, perhaps as part of a courtship ritual. It&#39;s hard to be sure with these birds, especially if you&#39;re me and lack practical experience in the field, but I think that the single black bar on the tail makes it most likely to be a Kestrel, having studied the book long and hard. If you think different, please let me know in the comments!</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f301875970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadKestrel 6485" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f301875970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f301875970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div>

<div>Some more photos of the range of interesting bird life at Rye Meads. See <a href="/atc/2009/04/rye-meads-rspb-reserve.html" target="_blank">my previous post</a> for the first instalment. There&#39;s still more to come, bit by bit. It took me a while to figure out what all these things were as I&#39;m new to it, so I don&#39;t want to swamp my dear readers (both of you) with too much at once.</div><br /><div>First, a twofer! That&#39;s two for the price of one. At the top a male Gadwall, and at the bottom a Coot collecting lumber for a nest I imagine. I have better pictures of both male and female Gadwalls to come in a later post.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570211c88970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadGadwallCoot 6477" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570211c88970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570211c88970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><br /><div>A Greylag Goose (actually a pair of them) flew overhead and I managed to get a half decent shot.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570211eb8970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadGreylagGoose 6480" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570211eb8970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570211eb8970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><br /><div>This taxed my powers of identification but I&#39;m now certain that it&#39;s a female Tufted Duck. Comparing it to the male in my previous post it&#39;s quite different, though there is a suggestion of tuftiness still.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f2aa746970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadTuftedDuckFemale 6479" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f2aa746970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f2aa746970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><br /><div>Finally a Pochard, which on the day I was confusing with Shovellers, but on reflection (picture pun intended) it&#39;s clearly quite different.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157021212d970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadPochard 6481" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157021212d970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157021212d970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Rye Meads RSPB Reserve
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: rye-meads-rspb-reserve
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Travel

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/rye-meads-rspb-reserve.html 
DATE: 04/13/2009 10:40:43 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Bank holiday Monday and it looked like the skies might clear for the first time in the long four day weekend. So off we drove to <a href="http://www.rspb.org.uk/reserves/guide/r/ryemeads/index.asp" target="_blank">Rye Meads wildlife reserve</a>&#0160;near Hoddesdon in Hertfordshire - a slightly strange split between RSPB and Herts and Middlesex Wildlife Trust. It&#39;s also right next to the Rye House go-karting track where Lewis Hamilton apparently learnt his craft, and I&#39;ve been karting many a time. So I can highly recommend this spot on at least two counts!</div><br /><div>Just a very quick report for now: it&#39;s a fairly large place with loads of lagoons, ditches, reed beds, ponds etc. and 11 hides from which to look out for interesting things. A couple of notables below (but more to come).</div><br /><div>First up, a lone Redshank. It took a bit of leafing through the book of longish billed wadey things to figure this one out.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f2217a1970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadRedshank 6472" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f2217a1970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f2217a1970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>&#0160;<br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Next, a tufted duck - this is a male. The female is all dark and without the obvious tufting on the back of the head.</div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570190026970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadTuftedDuck 6474" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570190026970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570190026970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">An Oystercatcher, with it&#39;s very colourful bill. Apologies for the poor sharpness of this photo. It was probably at least 50m away.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115701900cc970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadOysterCatcher 6475" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340115701900cc970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340115701900cc970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Finally, a Shoveller - a duck with a great big spade like bill. More interesting sights to come...</div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570190271970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="RyeMeadShoveller 6477" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570190271970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570190271970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div>

<div>It was a bit of a miserable grey day today, punctuated with rain. So an imperfect opportunity for a stroll to Verulam Park in St Albans, but we went anyway. There were herons with chicks to be seen on the island in the middle of the lake, a grebe, swallows swooping around and the usual less exotic bird life. Here are a couple of highlights. You can see two ugly Heron chicks in the nest below.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157014408f970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="HeronChicksVerulam 6410" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401157014408f970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401157014408f970b-500wi" style="width: 500px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f1d5f10970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="GrebeVerulam 6408" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f1d5f10970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f1d5f10970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570144108970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="HeronFlightVerulam 6409" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011570144108970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011570144108970b-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a> <br /></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: MobileMe Sync Problems
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: mobileme-sync-problems
CATEGORY: Mac

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/mobileme-sync-problems.html 
DATE: 04/07/2009 10:03:46 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;ve seen quite a number of MobileMe sync problems over the last couple of months. Some items that I add/delete/modify in my calendar don&#39;t make the jump between laptop and desktop, or vice versa. It&#39;s completely intermittent and always works perfectly if I deliberately try to catch it out. This is extremely unhelpful of it, as it means I just can&#39;t trust my calendar on either machine to be correct.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f08936e970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Picture 7" border="0" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156f08936e970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f08936e970c-800wi" title="Picture 7" /></a>&#0160;<a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156f08936e970c-pi" style="display: inline;"><br /></a></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div>I&#39;ve found a way to at least get things back in sync (as far as I can tell without doing an exhaustive comparison) though it remains to be seen whether it will actually stop things going wrong again. On all machines, open the iSync application, open its Preferences, then click the &quot;Reset sync history...&quot; button, and select &quot;Merge&quot; when asked. It might ask several times, for various types of sync data, with big spinny-arrow pauses in-between. You&#39;re putting yourself in the hands of MobileMe&#39;s sync algorithms here, so don&#39;t come crying to me if it makes your kettle fur up with scale and your cat leave home. I&#39;ll update this post as and when I determine if the more general problem is licked. Sadly my gut feeling is that it&#39;s too much to hope.</div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update:</span> Still been having problems and have been tearing my hair out. The recent OS X 10.5.7 update promises some sync improvements though isn&#39;t specific about what. I&#39;m hopeful, but I won&#39;t know for a couple of months as it usually picks just one or two important items to not sync every now and then. Enough to keep me on my toes.</div>
-----
EXTENDED BODY:

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: impaler

The best bet when you're having sync problems really isn't to "merge", but to take the latest copy from a desktop machine and replace ALL information in the cloud.  Then let it propogate back down.  Also, on each machine, you should reset the sync history.  That is the real problem.

-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;ve endeavoured to rely on NetBeans&#39; auto-formatting feature before now, but I&#39;ve never been able to configure it quite to my tastes. In particular I had struggled to get rules for braces and line wrapping setup how I wanted for Java, with them almost always on a new line. I&#39;m not sure whether I had simply failed to find the right options or if they&#39;re new in 6.5.1 or another recent release.</div><br /><div>It&#39;s perfect for cleaning up code that you&#39;re copying and pasting from the web, taking the labour out of it. All I need now in NetBeans is a less&#0160;tedious way of generating getters and setters that requires less mouse clicks, remembers your last preferences (put at end of file, no comments) and can have m_ prefix on the vars but not the accessor method names.</div><br /><div>That said, better still would be for Java to grow up a bit and gain a better way to do boiler plate accessors. Which is better:</div><br /><div>Java:</div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">public int getFoo()</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">{</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;return m_foo;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">}</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">public void setFoo(value)</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">{</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;m_foo = value;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">}</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">public String getBar()</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">{</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;return m_bar;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">}</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">public void setBar(value)</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">{</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;m_bar = value;</span></span></div><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">}</span></span></div><br /><div>Ruby:</div><br /><div><span style="font-size: 12px; "><span style="font-family: Courier;">attr_accessor :foo, :bar</span></span></div>

<div>I bought a pack of these unpretentious cans on holiday in Cornwall, as there wasn&#39;t much choice in the small Co-op. I could have had John Smiths Extra Smooth, but I&#39;m not a fan of the smooth creamy head on my pint. It&#39;s shame that Cornwall seems to be one of those areas that puts the cream-flow spouts on their draught beer taps, like they do up North. A classic way to ruin a perfectly good pint if you ask me.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156eed1f07970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="JohnSmithsOriginal 6348" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401156eed1f07970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401156eed1f07970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">There&#39;s not much to say for this very straightforward bitter - it&#39;s got good colour and a decent enough flavour, but it&#39;s very one-dimensional with pretty much zero in the way of extra interesting tastes coming through other than the basic bitterness. A good cheap beer to keep in for light mid-week requirements, but I find <a href="/atc/2008/11/beer-review-greene-king-ipa-can.html">Greene King IPA</a> fills that role better.</div>
