AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Adventures in Ruby 1.9.1
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: adventures-in-ruby-191
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/04/adventures-in-ruby-191.html 
DATE: 04/01/2009 10:17:27 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>Spurred on by <a href="http://antoniocangiano.com/2009/03/23/rubys-biggest-challenge-for-2009/" target="_blank">Antonio Cangiano&#39;s recent campaign</a>, I&#39;ve been trying to make a permanent move to Ruby 1.9.1. I&#39;ve been on 1.8.6 by default for a good while, as that&#39;s what you get with Mac OS X 10.5, though it&#39;s actually not too hard to get going compiling your own Ruby from source - no special tricks are required and it works great. I hope Apple makes 1.9.x the default for OS X 10.6 when it appears later this year. Go on - be brave!</div><br /><div>So what have I found in this exciting new world?</div><div><ul>
<li>It&#39;s a bit faster, as expected, though nothing to get especially excited about.</li>
<li><span>Some very popular gems just don&#39;t work straight off the shelf. Mongrel for instance - though I&#39;m told it&#39;s really easy to get it working with a couple of tweaks. Still, it&#39;s a shame for the sake of the community that the official latest gem can&#39;t have those tweaks made to it and save a lot of people an awful lot of fuss. Actually I hear that Mongrel 2 is on the way, taking advantage of more of Rack&#39;s infrastructure. I look forward to it.</span>&#0160;</li>
<li>ri now works really quickly compared to my 1.8.6 install, which always thinks for a couple of seconds before giving me a result. I think this is due to a new caching mechanism that was introduced around 1.8.7. Simple things like this make a huge difference to my life!</li>
<li><span>ri actually finds docs for the standard libraries, which it didn&#39;t for my 1.8.7 install. I think was due to some issue in the standard makefile for 1.8.7, but I&#39;m not really sure. Again, for someone like me who only just knows what he&#39;s doing, this makes a huge difference.</span></li>
<li><span>Proper string encoding support! Finally the promised land, as long as you can take the hit of string indexing now being incredibly slow.</span></li>
<li><span>Rubygems at 1.3.1, which is much more refined than that bundled with ruby 1.8.6 on Mac OS X. You can get this for yourself on any version of Ruby with gem update --system BTW.</span>&#0160;No more &quot;<span style="font-family: arial; line-height: normal; ">Bulk updating Gem source&quot;.</span></li>
</ul>
<span>I recommend everyone tries to move on up, at least with a parallel install that they can easily switch, and applies pressure to the holdouts to get it happening. Commercial pressures will make it tough for many, but when we break on through to the other side it will all be worth it I reckon.</span>&#0160;<br /></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Install Ruby 1.9.1 on Windows
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: install-ruby-191-on-windows
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/install-ruby-191-on-windows.html 
DATE: 03/31/2009 08:24:04 AM
-----
BODY:
<p><strong>Update:</strong>&#0160;There&#0160;is&#0160;a&#0160;new&#0160;effort&#0160;to&#0160;create&#0160;a&#0160;decent&#0160;Windows&#0160;Ruby&#0160;installer. You can get the new downloadable installers from&#0160;<a href="http://rubyinstaller.org/" target="_blank">http://rubyinstaller.org/</a> though as of August 2009 these are still in preview form. It seems from my brief experiment that you&#39;ll still have to set your PATH manually, as per my manual instructions below. This installer also promises a faster Ruby as it builds using superior tools, so might well be worthwhile if only for that. Below are my original manual instructions for installing Ruby on Windows, which should still be just as valid as they always were. The installer is probably worth a look though.</p>

<p>The following worked for me on Windows XP, but maybe I&#39;m yet to run into problems I don&#39;t know I&#39;ve got:</p>

<p></p>

<ul>
<li>Download the Windows binary release from&#0160;<a href="http://www.ruby-lang.org/en/downloads/" target="_blank">http://www.ruby-lang.org/en/downloads/</a></li>
<li>Unzip it, in my case to C:\ruby191\</li>
<li><span>Also download readline.dll (e.g. from <a href="http://www.econsultant.com/dll-database-r/readline.dll-download.html" target="_blank">http://www.econsultant.com/dll-database-r/readline.dll-download.html</a>) and put the DLL in C:\ruby191\bin</span></li>
<li><span>Put C:\ruby191\bin and C:\ruby191\lib on your PATH</span></li>
</ul>
<span>That got ruby working in a basic sense for me, but I wasn&#39;t able to install gems. If I tried, I got two errors in sequence:&#0160;&quot;</span><span style="font-style: italic;">The ordinal 277 could not be located in the dynamic link library SSLEAY32.dll</span><span>&quot; and &quot;</span><span style="font-style: italic;">This application has failed to start because zlib.dll was not found.</span><span>&quot;</span><p></p>

<br /><p>The ssleay32.dll problem was solved by getting hold of a couple of DLLs and putting them in the right place, as follows:</p>

<p></p>

<ul>
<li>Download &quot;Win32 OpenSSL v0.9.8k Light&quot; from <a href="http://www.slproweb.com/products/Win32OpenSSL.html" target="_blank">http://www.slproweb.com/products/Win32OpenSSL.html</a>.</li>
<li><span>Run the installer, but take note of the following:</span></li>
<li>You can ignore any initial warning about not having Microsoft Visual C++ 2008 Redistributables.</li>
<li>You can ignore any warnings about command prompts being open.</li>
<li><span>Install to somewhere like C:\OpenSSL - it doesn&#39;t really matter, we just need a couple of DLLs.</span></li>
<li><span>When prompted, choose to Copy OpenSSL DLLS to The OpenSSL binaries (/bin) directory.</span></li>
<li><span>Once the installer has completed, you can go to C:\OpenSSL\bin (or wherever you installed it) and copy the two files ssleay32.dll and libeay32.dll to your Ruby bin directory, C:\ruby191\bin in my case.</span></li>
</ul>
<span>The zlib.dll problem was solved by in much the same way:</span><p></p>

<p></p>

<ul>
<li>Download the compiled zlib 1.2.3 DLL from&#0160;<a href="http://www.zlib.net/" target="_blank">http://www.zlib.net/</a> (about two thirds of the way down the page).</li>
<li><span>Unzip it to get the zlib1.dll file.</span></li>
<li><span>Put that file in your Ruby bin directory, but rename it to zlib.dll</span></li>
</ul>
<span>Et voila - gems can now be installed successfully! Note that you may have had copies of some of these DLLs in your \WINDOWS\system32 directory already (I certainly did) but the problem is that they are too old. I prefer to put the newer ones directly in the Ruby bin directory, so only Ruby picks them up and it can&#39;t break anything else on the system.</span><p></p>
-----

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Sam3
EMAIL: 
IP: 217.20.31.166
URL: http://profile.typepad.com/1220903235s28378
DATE: 10/05/2009 04:42:57 PM
You need to set the Windows environment variable PATH to include the directory where the Ruby binaries are installed. This PATH lists the directories that Windows should search when trying to find an executable, so that (for instance) when you type "ruby someapp.rb" on the command line, it knows where the 'ruby' executable is. I suggest that you Google for "setting windows PATH environment" or similar.

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Mac Bluetooth fail
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: mac-bluetooth-fail
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/mac-bluetooth-fail.html 
DATE: 03/28/2009 04:24:04 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>Rather mysteriously, my bluetooth mouse suddenly stopped responding with no warning. I assumed it was duff batteries, though I&#39;d normally get a warning a good while before they conk out completely. Changed them, but still no dice. I attached a wired mouse and did some digging - basically Bluetooth no longer seemed to exist on my Mac! The menubar widget claimed bluetooth was &#39;not available&#39; and the System Preferences Panel said similar. Restarting didn&#39;t help - in fact it made the menubar widget and System Preferences Panel disappear entirely!</p><br /><div>After a bit of digging on the web, what worked for me was to reset PRAM. Shutdown computer, then start it up whilst holding down cmd-option-P-R until it chimes a second time. Actually I held down until a third chime for good luck. Seems to have done the trick!</div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update:</span> It then failed again shortly afterwards. Next move was to delete a critical prefs file. Best not to do this unless you have a clue what it means:</div><div><ul>
<li>sudo mv /var/root/Library/Preferences/blued.plist ~/Desktop/</li>
</ul>
At the same time I shutdown the computer, removed all attached cables, opened it up and pressed the PMU/SMC reset button down near the RAM (this is a PowerMac G5 tower) then restarted. It seems to be OK for now. It did mean the clock resetting to 1970 and me having to wait for all my iCal notifications to stream in once I&#39;d reset to 2009.<br /><br /></div><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update 2:</span> And it&#39;s gone again. Maybe it&#39;ll never be back. I swear if Apple sold some sort of mid-tower machine - somewhere between an iMac (but without a screen) and the Mac Pro, I&#39;d buy one without a second&#39;s hesitation. I&#39;ve been waiting for years for this machine, on the assumption that it&#39;s an obvious hole in their line-up and will be along any moment, but I&#39;ve been hideously disappointed.</div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update 3:</span> It seems that if I shutdown the computer rather than simply restarting, my bluetooth comes back to life. At the moment it&#39;s been fine for all of today, but it might flake out on me at a moment&#39;s notice for all I know.</div><br /><div><span style="font-weight: bold;">Update 4</span>: Correction - resetting PRAM by holding down cmd-opt-P-R until you hear a second chime is vital. Simply shutting down and restarting does not seem to do it. At the moment I sometimes get a few days of successful use between failures.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Install mysql gem on Ruby 1.9.1 on Mac OS X
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: install-mysql-gem-on-ruby-191-on-mac-os-x
CATEGORY: Mac
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/install-mysql-gem-on-ruby-191-on-mac-os-x.html 
DATE: 03/28/2009 03:19:55 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve been experimenting with Ruby 1.9.1 and trying to make it my natural home recently. Crucial to that was getting the mysql gem installed. Actually it&#39;s fairly straightforward with the 2.8.1 version of the gem and only very minor mods to the official instructions at&#0160;<a href="http://www.tmtm.org/en/mysql/ruby/" target="_blank">http://www.tmtm.org/en/mysql/ruby/</a> were required.</p><br /><div>Note that I have only tried this to work against MySQL 5.0.x, which I have installed in /usr/local/mysql, as all my previous attempts to use MySQL 5.1 have failed miserably and I&#39;ve given up on that for now. These instructions install from source, resulting in a fresh&#0160;/usr/local/ruby191/lib/ruby/site_ruby/1.9.1/mysql.bundle. Then you can simply require &#39;mysql&#39; in your Ruby code and you&#39;re away.</div><br /><div><div><ul>
<li>Downloaded 2.8.1 source tgz from RubyForge, linked from <a href="http://www.tmtm.org/en/mysql/ruby" target="_blank">http://www.tmtm.org/en/mysql/ruby</a>/ downloads section.</li>
<li>Expanded the tgz with Finder, then opened a command prompt...</li>
<li>&gt; cd mysql-ruby-2.8.1</li>
<li>&gt; ruby extconf.rb --with-mysql-config=/usr/local/mysql/bin/mysql_config -with-mysql-dir=/usr/local/mysql</li>
<li>&gt; make</li>
<li>&gt; sudo make install</li>
</ul>
</div><div>Et voila! Note that make did output a bunch of warnings about implicit 64/32 bit conversion that suggests the code isn&#39;t entirely 64 bit happy, but I&#39;ve not observed any problems as a result. Yet.</div><br /></div>
-----
EXTENDED BODY:

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Read some code today!
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: 0
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: read-some-code-today
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/read-some-code-today.html 
DATE: 03/24/2009 10:32:49 PM
-----
BODY:
I advise every programmer out there to read lots of other people's code. Pick an open source project that you're a fan of (and perhaps use every day), download the source and then sit and try and figure out how it works. You may find the following things:<div><ul>
<li>It's interesting to see how other people write code, from their specific syntactic style to the level of commenting, overall design and code layout, common idioms etc. You'll probably learn something - either good things that you can copy, or bad things that are tough to read and warn you off doing the same.</li>
<li><span>You may find the code frustrating and difficult to figure out, not only initially but no matter who long you work through it, though if that's the case then it's probably poor code. Hopefully that will influence how you write your own code, for the better.</span>&nbsp;</li>
<li>If it is a product that you use regularly, you may gain insights into what it's really doing and how, that empower you to use it more effectively. You may even be able to modify it to make some improvement that matters to you.</li>
<li>Once you've got past the initial difficulty and started to understand things, you acquire a sense of power. No longer are these complicated pieces of software opaque and mysterious. They can be broken down, understood and messed with. A whole world of possibilities opens up and you no longer need to be afraid.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>
<span>I've recently been reading the <a href="http://github.com/manveru/innate/tree/master" target="_blank">Ruby source code of Innate</a> - the new underpinnings of next generation <a href="http://ramaze.net" target="_blank">Ramaze</a>,&nbsp;a Ruby web app framework that I particularly enjoy using. Innate is built on top of <a href="http://rack.rubyforge.org/" target="_blank">Rack</a>, so I've been reading that too. The mystery is ebbing away and it's making me more powerful. Stand back! :-)</span><br></div>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Non-stick non-tasty
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: nonstick-nontasty
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/nonstick-nontasty.html 
DATE: 03/23/2009 08:47:26 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>It&#39;s easy to just pull out the big non-stick half-wok half-frying pan thing for most meals that involve frying anything (especially if you&#39;re going to add liquid later as its high sides mean a good overall capacity), but it&#39;s not always best. A good stainless steel thick-bottomed frying pan has some important qualities that you&#39;re missing out on. In particular you&#39;ll be missing out on building a layer of tasty brown fried-on stuff as you do your onions and meat, which you can then de-glaze into the sauce as you add liquid. In a non-stick pan that just doesn&#39;t really happen.
</p>
<p>We got a great deal on a set of Prestige stainless steel cookware a couple of years back and it&#39;s been a real eye opener. My biggest worry was that it would be a complete pain to wash up, but this really isn&#39;t the case at all. Generally things don&#39;t stick much anyway, and a short soak soon sorts out even the worst offence. And they look dead professional :-)</p>

TITLE: FCC Thameslink: bigger trains, except maybe not

<div>I was overjoyed to see the new March 22nd First Capital Connect Thameslink timetable, which seems to have a straight flush of 8 car trains from St Albans into London in the morning rush hour! There are also more 8 car trains coming back out in the evening than there were, but they don&#39;t seem to have managed to cover all services.</div>
<br />
<div>That would have been it for this post, but my wife told me that she&#39;d had a letter from FCC saying that the new trains required to do this are actually four months late (cue obvious jokes) and so we&#39;ll be lucky to actually see this working out in reality. Either that or they will try to manage it with yet more borrowed stock from Southern, but cunningly setup such that the first minor problem wrecks everything through lack of contingency capacity. Brilliant.</div>
<br />
<div>I still find it highly amusing that there&#39;s all this talk of 12 car trains when they still can&#39;t run anything better than 4 car trains for many rush hour services. Let&#39;s get to 8 first!</div>
<br />
<div>Incidentally, is there an even more disgruntled commuter somewhere in the deep South wondering why they now have 1 car trains, by virtue of all Southern&#39;s stock having been sent to FCC? Or did they just have all these trains sat in a shed waiting for their calling?!</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Cornwall: Eden Project
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: cornwall-eden-project
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Travel

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/cornwall-eden-project.html 
DATE: 03/20/2009 10:11:24 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>One of the things in Cornwall that you have to visit by law is the Eden Project. It&#39;s quite pricey to get in (notably more than the Lost Gardens of Heligan for instance) and dare I say that rankled a bit. It&#39;s reputation precedes it, so it had a lot to live up to. To be honest it fell a little short, but that might be because we&#39;d been to Heligan just two days before which had set a high bar, and our expectation were a bit unrealistically set from all the media coverage that it gets.</p><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279840d1228a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="EdenProjectBiomes 6317" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011279840d1228a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279840d1228a4-450wi" style="width: 450px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">The two biomes are genuinely impressive and by far the best thing about the place. Set in an old quarry, they are sheltered from the elements and act as enormous greenhouses with the larger set housing a tropical climate and the other a cooler&#0160;Mediterranean&#0160;experience. It was a super-sunny day for March and the tropical biome was absolutely scorching. Sweat was dripping down every part of me. It was almost a chore, save for the extraordinary wealth of lush tropicana all around, including waterfalls, streams and millions of tiny ants. A member of staff told me that the ants and all the birdlife weren&#39;t really supposed to be there but there wasn&#39;t much they could do. I watched a robin catching a grasshopper, and another one eating a large cockroach! With those sorts of goods on offer I&#39;m not surprised they wait to come through the sliding doors with the rest of the visitors.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279841fe328a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="EdenProjectTropicalBiome 6316" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011279841fe328a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279841fe328a4-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a>
 &#0160;<a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279841de528a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="EdenProjectWEEEMan 6315" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011279841de528a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279841de528a4-300wi" style="width: 300px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Above on the right is a picture of WEEE Man (if I recall correctly) - a huge sculpture made of the electrical waste that the average person throws away in a lifetime. Those teeth are computer mice.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340112798424fd28a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="EdenProjectCore 6318" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340112798424fd28a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340112798424fd28a4-500wi" style="width: 500px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">The&#0160;Mediterranean&#0160;biome was probably my favourite bit, as it was a pleasant place to be in absolutely every sense, though not quite as vast and absorbing as the tropics. One building is known as The Core and at the centre of it is a 75 tonne sculpture hewn from a single piece of rock - apparently the largest single stone ever quarried in the UK. It&#39;s an egg or pine-cone shaped sculpture with highly geometric qualities that represents a seed pod at the core of the whole endeavour. I kind of liked it, but the building it was in, full of educational stuff, didn&#39;t quite seem to gel.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">Overall, if you&#39;re going to Cornwall you&#39;d be mad not to visit the Eden project, but if it were much busier than it was in mid-March it might be a bit of a struggle. I think I&#39;d recommend people go, but I&#39;d even more strongly recommend they go to the Lost Gardens of Heligan nearby. More on that to come.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Rook vs Crow
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: rook-vs-crow
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Travel

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/rook-vs-crow.html 
DATE: 03/19/2009 07:32:10 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve always struggled to tell the difference between Rook, Crow and Raven, though I&#39;ve had Jackdaws sorted for years (notably smaller - black cap above more slightly silvery head and neck). To remedy this situation I&#39;ve really been trying to get to grips with the differences and positively identify large black birds over recent months. Here&#39;s what I&#39;m fairly sure is a rook, on a feeder at the Lost Gardens of Heligan in Cornwall.</p><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340112797c1fbb28a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Rook 6315" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340112797c1fbb28a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340112797c1fbb28a4-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><br /><div>This is fairly classicly an adult rook by virtue of the grey area around the base of the bill, which itself is long and pointy compared to that of a carrion crow. That said, the head shape seems more typically crow-like to me (less of a crown) and in this example there&#39;s no evidence of the &#39;baggy trousers&#39; that usually distinguishes a rook. As a result I&#39;m left still fairly puzzled, but I&#39;m going with my call that this is a rook. Furthermore, there were lots of them around all together and apparently this is a sign of rookedness.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Cornwall: Port Isaac
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: cornwall-port-isaac
CATEGORY: Photography
CATEGORY: Travel

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/cornwall-port-isaac.html 
DATE: 03/18/2009 10:24:16 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;m back! And this time I&#39;m wearing an &quot;I&#39;ve been to Cornwall&quot; t-shirt - metaphorically speaking of course.</p><p>We just stayed for 5 days in Port Isaac on the North coast of Cornwall, between Padstow (amusingly named Padstein by some) and Tintagel. Our home for those few days of blissful weather was The Birdcage - a National Trust&#0160;holiday&#0160;cottage right in the heart of Port Isaac, literally stumbling distance from the pub. You might recognise Port Isaac from the ITV comedy/drama Doc Marting, starring Martin Clunes, though I understand it&#39;s been used for lots of other things too, because it&#39;s such a charming little spot.</p><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401116901f74e970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="PortIssacWide 6265" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e0158883401116901f74e970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e0158883401116901f74e970c-500wi" style="width: 500px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">You can just about see The Birdcage about half-way up above the red van in the image above, but there&#39;s a closer shot below. It&#39;s a pentagonal building with just a single room on each floor, bar the bathroom tacked onto the ground floor as an extension. Straight in off the street (though you&#39;d struggle to drive up a three foot wide 30 degree path) you&#39;re into the kitchen - compact but bijou. Then up a very steep, very narrow staircase to the lounge and then an even narrower staircase (about 15 inches across) to the bedroom. It was absolutely delightful, but the only way you can truly understand is to stay there yourself. I can recommend it.</div><div style="text-align: left;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279760c5928a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="PortIssacBirdcage 6263" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011279760c5928a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011279760c5928a4-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>

TITLE: MacRuby 0.4

BASENAME: macruby-04

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/macruby-04.html 
DATE: 03/10/2009 10:07:06 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve been following the progress of MacRuby for a few months now and was looking forward to the next release. Finally, and a month or three after it was expected, MacRuby 0.4 is here, according to <a href="http://www.macruby.org/news/" target="_blank" title="MacRuby blog">the MacRuby blog</a>.</p><p>MacRuby is, to keep it simple, an implementation of Ruby 1.9 built in Objective-C with transparent calling between the ruby and Obj-C worlds. For instance Ruby Strings are actually NSStrings in MacRuby and have all the powers of both. This along with some other neat integrations provided by the team allow you to write Mac OS X Cocoa apps using Ruby instead of Obj-C, or even a combination of both. If like me you think that Obj-C is a dinosaur that doesn&#39;t deserve a place in the modern programming world, this is great news. There seems to be a lot of momentum behind this project and I wonder if it may eventually become quite a fully fledged citizen for Mac app programmers - maybe even ultimately taking over from Obj-C for most Mac OS X development. I can but hope.</p><p>One of the many neat improvements in 0.4 is the ability to bundle the MacRuby runtime into the app itself so the user need be none the wiser, and certainly doesn&#39;t need to install MacRuby.</p>
-----

<p>I tried doing rice a bit differently to normal this evening, following <a href="http://www.deliaonline.com/cookery-school/how-to/how-to-cook-perfect-rice,1,AR.html" target="_blank" title="Delia&#39;s perfect rice">Delia&#39;s approach</a>. But it often clumps together on the plate and I thought it must be possible to do better.</p><br /><div>Delia&#39;s approach involves a large covered saucepan, no rinsing, just enough water, almost no stirring and a couple of important other tips. Not having my usual rice alongside to compare with made it hard to know whether it was much of an improvement. I&#39;d post a picture of my result, but it would be really very dull.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: ActiveRecord: Validations and Source Reload
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: activerecord-validations-and-source-reload
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/03/activerecord-validations-and-source-reload.html 
DATE: 03/03/2009 07:42:53 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I was running into an annoyance whereby I&#39;d end up with duplicated validation messages from ActiveRecord. I tracked this down to the source reload feature of <a href="http://ramaze.net" target="_blank" title="Ramaze - Ruby web application framework">Ramaze</a>, which was reloading my AR model classes each time I edited them with the dev server running, which has the unfortunate side effect of adding the validators again on top of the same ones that were there before. So each reload adds another set of validators and another duplicate validation message. This sort of thing (mainly class methods that add to a list) often requires special care when working with a source reloading system. In this case I&#39;ve worked around it by resetting AR&#39;s internal validator list to empty before creating my validators, by directly accessing the&#0160;@validate_callbacks instance variable. For example:</p><br /><div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">class Event &lt; ActiveRecord::Base</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;# Clear all AR validations, to avoid getting duplicates on source reload.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;@validate_callbacks = []</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;validates_presence_of :name</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;validates_presence_of :description</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">end</span></div></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: ActiveRecord - much maligned, but actually OK
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: activerecord-much-maligned-but-actually-ok
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/activerecord-much-maligned-but-actually-ok.html 
DATE: 02/23/2009 10:53:41 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve been furiously porting my Ramaze CRUD helper from <a href="http://datamapper.org/" target="_blank" title="DataMapper Ruby ORM">DataMapper</a> to <a href="http://ar.rubyonrails.com/" target="_blank" title="ActiveRecord Ruby ORM">ActiveRecord</a> and I&#39;ve been quite surprised to find that it&#39;s only taken a couple of hours. I should hastily point out for non-programmer types, that CRUD stands for Create Read Update Delete - the four basic operations you can perform on a data entity.</p><br /><div>My helper makes it super-easy to produce basic CRUD web pages for any data entity, with just a couple of lines of code in your helper - a bit like Rails scaffolds but without the scaffolding! Sounds like magic, I hear you yell! Well yes, but it&#39;s my magic that I wrote from scratch so it&#39;s not magic to me :-)<br /><div>It&#39;s been an easy port between ORMs mostly because the concepts and syntax map over with relatively little fuss, and actually 98% of my CRUD helper code is not concerned with the DB, but rather with running the web UI. So the few places that actually perform a DB operation needed a bit of tweaking to use the right syntax, but it&#39;s been pretty smooth sailing. Hopefully it will be easier with AR to neatly deal with many-to-many associations without having to explicitly fool around with the join table. We shall see, as that&#39;s what foxed me with DataMapper and caused me to switch.</div><br /><div>One very important thing to mention is that when using ActiveRecord outside of Rails you have to know that it was originally written for the single-threaded Rails world. Hence it just maintains a very small connection pool (say 3) and those connections quickly run out in a threaded environment - Ramaze in my case. This leads to strange pauses of several seconds as your request handling thread waits for a connection to come free. They come free after about 5 seconds of inactivity, so you get one reasonably soon, but the multi-second pause is infuriating and very confusing. A neatly packaged solution is handily presented and well explained here:&#0160;<a href="http://coderrr.wordpress.com/2009/01/16/monkey-patching-activerecord-to-automatically-release-connections/" target="_blank">http://coderrr.wordpress.com/2009/01/16/monkey-patching-activerecord-to-automatically-release-connections/</a>. Note that there is a link at the bottom to the single-file patch that just sorts everything out for you, so if you can&#39;t be bothered to read the explanations, head straight for that, require it in your project and you&#39;re away.</div></div>
-----

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Google App Engine - a better RequestHandler
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: google-app-engine-a-better-requesthandler
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/google-app-engine-a-better-requesthandler.html 
DATE: 02/18/2009 10:57:40 PM
-----
BODY:
<div>I&#39;ve developed a very simple webapp.RequestHandler subclass that does a couple of useful things that I need in my GAE app. They seem like pretty basic things that are worth sharing - though anyone could have done it themselves in five minutes.</div><br /><div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">class BBRequestHandler(webapp.RequestHandler):</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;# Render a template with standard data mixed in.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;def render(self, template_name, template_data = {}):</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;template_data.update(self.sundries())</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;self.response.out.write(template.render(&#39;view/&#39; + template_name, template_data))</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;# Forward the request to a different handler. Assumes GET.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;def forward(self, handler_class):</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;handler = handler_class()</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;handler.initialize(self.request, self.response)</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;handler.get()</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;# Standard template data required for most pages.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;def sundries(self):</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;return {<span style="font-family: &#39;Trebuchet MS&#39;; ">&#39;logout&#39;:users.create_logout_url(&#39;/&#39;)<span style="font-family: Courier; ">}</span></span></span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></div><div>The intention is that sundries() should return whatever standard data your pages require that&#39;s not specific to individual pages. In my case that&#39;s data that&#39;s required for the header, footer and sidebar - like the logout URL.</div><br /><div>You may notice that I&#39;m not using the Python docstring format. I just can&#39;t quite bring myself to do that just yet, though I have got used to the indentation-is-block thing.</div></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Beer Review: Kasteel Cru
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: beer-review-kasteel-cru
CATEGORY: Beer
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/beer-review-kasteel-cru.html 
DATE: 02/15/2009 04:51:26 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>This is quite a special lager - brewed with champagne yeast it has a very fine, light bubble and it does indeed taste like a beery champagne. Imagine a very fine champagne with the sweetness replaced with dry pils bitterness. Certainly the lingering feel in the mouth is more champagne than beer.</p><div>There are very few beers brewed in this style, so this one from Alsace is quite unique and worth tracking down. I was spurred to pick some up because Waitrose had 25% of the bottles - and it was on taste :-) It&#39;s really very good indeed - super refreshing and drinkable (but remember it&#39;s a deceptive 5.2% ABV) but a bit unusual.</div><br /><div>In this case I was drinking it from a&#0160;Kölsch glass, which isn&#39;t entirely right, but seemed to suit it&#39;s delicacy. I suppose it could/should be drunk from a champagne flute!</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011278db551228a4-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="KasteelCru" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834011278db551228a4 " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834011278db551228a4-350wi" style="width: 350px; " /></a>

<p>I&#39;ve noticed that a lot of the standard Python methods on lists and dictionaries don&#39;t return anything at all - they simply modify self and return nothing.</p><div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt;&gt;&gt; l = [1,2,3]</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt;&gt;&gt; r = l.reverse()</span></div><div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt;&gt;&gt; print l</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">[3, 2, 1]</span></div></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&gt;&gt;&gt; print r</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">None</span></div><br /><div>This is becoming extremely frustrating as it prevents chaining of method calls, which would be possible if only self was returned. AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Google App Engine - Query[] and Query.count()
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: google-app-engine-query-and-querycount
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/google-app-engine-query-and-querycount.html 
DATE: 02/11/2009 11:09:24 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I ran into an interesting performance issue with my Google App Engine application, which I&#39;m currently developing feverishly. It seems that if you use indexed array style access to an unfetched Query object, performance is atrocious. I&#39;m fairly sure that it&#39;s doing a fetch for each access. It will also hit the &#39;DB&#39; if you call count() on the Query object. Far superior is to explicitly fetch() from the Query to get a list, then use that. It&#39;s all fairly obvious really, but it took me a while to realise what was going on, perhaps because you can innocuously use the Query object as an iterator without this problem, which lulls you into a false sense of security.</p><br /><div>So to give a contrived (and untested) example, this I would expect to be terrible performance wise:</div><br /><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">special_days = SpecialDay.all().filter(&#39;date &gt;=&#39;, start_date).order(&#39;date&#39;)</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">day = date.today()</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">end_date = day + timedelta(21)</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">sd_index = 0</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">while day &lt;= end_date:</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;# This count() call and the indexed access to special_days each cause a DB hit!</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;if special_days.count() &gt;&#0160;sd_index&#0160;and day == special_days[sd_index]:</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;print &#39;special day&#39;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;special_days_index += 1</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;else:</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;print &#39;ordinary day&#39;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;day += timedelta(1)</span></div><br /><div>It&#39;s easily fixed by simply adding fetch(1000) on the end of the first line, and using len(special_days) instead of special_days.count(). Also, having moved to working with a list, you can remove sd_index entirely and pop() items off the front of special_days until it&#39;s empty instead. It&#39;s a pain that there isn&#39;t a less skanky way to fetch &#39;all of them&#39; without using that nasty 1000. I suppose at the very least I should create a MAX_FETCH_LIMIT constant for it in my own code, so I can centrally modify it when Google modify their max limit.<br /><br /><div>Why can&#39;t I just use an iterator anyway you may ask? Because my loop, which is similar in structure to the example above, is iterating over days between two dates and each time round the loop picking the item off the front of my list of data entities (which were fetched in date order) if it matches the day. It&#39;s all working very nicely now thanks, and I&#39;ve added caching too, to save hitting the DB at all in many cases.</div></div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Mortgage Woes
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: mortgage-woes
CATEGORY: Current Affairs
CATEGORY: Me

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/mortgage-woes.html 
DATE: 02/10/2009 08:41:36 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>A funny thing appears to be happening in the mortgage market, and it&#39;s of precisely no help to me, seeing as I just want to buy a house to live in. First some facts to set the scene.</p><div><ul>
<li>Bank of England base rate here in the UK is down at 1%.</li>
<li>But you can&#39;t get a new mortgage with a rate any better than 5% if you only have a 15% deposit. That&#39;s the same rate you could get just 2 years ago, when the BoE base rate was 5.25% as far as I recall.</li>
<li>15% is a not unreasonable deposit by standards of recent years, and it&#39;s certainly a huge chunk of cash given the astronomical price of a three bed semi these days.</li>
<li>If you have a 40% deposit, you can get a new mortgage with a 2.29% rate.&#0160;</li>
<li>The price of houses is by the way still about double what it was just ten short years ago.</li>
</ul>
The net result of this is that people with vast stacks of money just sitting around in savings accounts are realising that they&#39;re not getting any interest on their stash and might be better off investing in bricks and mortar. Seeing as they have these giant piles of cash they can pay a 40% deposit no problem and take advantage of the seriously cheap mortgage deals available only to the already mega-rich. I&#39;m told by local estate agents that young folk are now streaming into their offices with their parents in tow (they supply the briefcase full of gold bars) to get their investment property sorted as a nice little earner.</div><br /><div>And guess what this influx does? Pushes up property prices, keeping the money-go-round spinning for those lucky enough to be on it. So, I put forward the bold statement that the decrease in base rates combined with the existing lack of credit is actually shutting non-investors out of the market. Which is really annoying because I was hoping for a cheap-houses-for-all feeding frenzy of which I could be a part :-(</div><br /><div>Of course, I&#39;m just bitter.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Google App Engine: Data Duplicity
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: google-app-engine-data-duplicity
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/google-app-engine-data-duplicity.html 
DATE: 02/05/2009 10:52:02 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve stumbled across a real irritation with the way the Google App Engine data model works. One among many frankly, but I&#39;ll restrict myself to just this one for now.</p><div><br /><div>The issue stems from the fact that db.run_in_transaction(func) insists that func is a function with no side effects, since it may be run repeatedly in an attempt to get the transaction to go through (if optimistic locking fails). Fair enough, but that means it has to freshly fetch any model objects that it wants to modify, otherwise it would have side effects to objects outside its scope. But consider this situation, in which we have an increment() function on our model object, that must use a transaction because it also modifies other related objects at the same time and require atomic behaviour:</div><div><br /><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">class Person(db.Model):</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;count = db.IntegerProperty(default=0, required=True)</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160;def increment(self):</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;def tx():</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160; &#0160;# Mess with some other related objects in data store.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160; &#0160;&lt;omitted for brevity&gt;</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160; &#0160;# Must fetch a separate copy of self to avoid side effects.</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160; &#0160;person = db.get(self.key())</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160; &#0160;person.count += 1</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160; &#0160;person.put()</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;">&#0160;&#0160; &#0160;db.run_in_transaction(tx)</span></div><div><span style="font-family: Courier;"><br /></span></div><div>The problem here is that self hasn&#39;t actually been modified at all and is now out of date with respect to the data store (where the count is one bigger, assuming the transaction succeeded). This is a pain for the caller who had a Person object and called increment() on it and naturally expects their object&#39;s count to be one higher. But their object hasn&#39;t been modified at all - though the data store has, via the freshly fetched person. In case it&#39;s not obvious, we can&#39;t simply change the code above to use self instead of getting the new person object, since&#0160;db.run_in_transaction(tx)&#0160;may run our tx() function multiple times until it completes without an optimistic locking failure. If it did have to run multiple times, self&#39;s count would increment by one for each failed attempt, so the final successful attempt could end up with more than one added to the count. Or if the transaction eventually failed outright, self&#39;s count would still have been modified even though the data store had not been touched.</div><br /><div>So the only solutions I can see are:</div><div><ul>
<li>Put code after the run_in_transaction() call, that synchronises self with the data store. There isn&#39;t a sync() or refresh() method on Model objects, so you have to do this painstakingly by getting another fresh person with&#0160;db.get(self.key()) and then copying across just the fields you know might have changed.</li>
<li>Insist that the caller is aware that certain methods on the model objects won&#39;t modify the object itself so they need to get a fresh one. This completely wrecks the idea of an object model and encapsulation though. You&#39;d might as well just have a purely functional interface to the data store.</li>
</ul>
</div><div>It all seems like madness to me, that defeats the point of trying to have a neat, simple data storage object model. As usual, I can only hope that I&#39;ve missed some crucial point and that in fact the problem is easily and elegantly solved. I shall look out for that solution, unless some kind reader can enlighten me!</div></div></div>
-----
EXTENDED BODY:

-----
EXCERPT:

-----
KEYWORDS:

-----
COMMENT:
AUTHOR: brett
EMAIL: 
IP: 72.14.229.81
URL: http://profile.typepad.com/brettalicious
DATE: 02/06/2009 02:24:31 AM
Use a piece of this to define sync() for yourself, and you should be good to go. Just make sure to do sync() after the transaction finishes (using a closure, or something):


class MyModel(db.Model):
  foo = db.IntegerProperty(default=0)

  def add_one(self):
    python_scoping_is_annoying = [None]
    def txn():
      new = python_scoping_is_annoying[0] = db.get(self.key())
      new.foo += 1
      new.put()
    db.run_in_transaction(txn)
    
    new = python_scoping_is_annoying[0]
    # Copy all new values over to this instance.
    for k in new.properties().keys() + new.dynamic_properties():
      setattr(self, k, getattr(new, k))



a = MyModel()
a.put()
print a.key(), a.foo
a.add_one()
print a.key(), a.foo
a.add_one()
print a.key(), a.foo



Output:

agVzaGVsbHIPCxIHTXlNb2RlbBjKqwUM 0
agVzaGVsbHIPCxIHTXlNb2RlbBjKqwUM 1
agVzaGVsbHIPCxIHTXlNb2RlbBjKqwUM 2



-----
COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Sam
EMAIL: samcarr@mac.com
IP: 86.155.210.138
URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com
DATE: 02/06/2009 07:51:21 AM
Thanks for the neat property sync code - it should definitely be useful to make the general approach more robust. It's still a long way from pretty though :-)

COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Pete
EMAIL: 
IP: 72.14.229.81
URL: http://profile.typepad.com/6p0111684f01f1970c
DATE: 02/06/2009 10:12:20 PM
A shorter but less generic way to do this is just to add a one-line property sync after the put() call:

def tx():
      # Mess with some other related objects in data store.
      
      # Must fetch a separate copy of self to avoid side effects.
      person = db.get(self.key())
      person.count += 1
      person.put()
      self.count = person.count
-----
COMMENT:
AUTHOR: Sam
EMAIL: samcarr@mac.com
IP: 86.155.210.138
URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com
DATE: 02/06/2009 11:27:10 PM
Pete - whether that works or not is dependent on the way the transactionality is implemented by Google. If it stacks up the datastore operations then attempts to commit after the whole of tx() has run, but then fails, you'll already have modified self, and that modification will live on even though the datastore wasn't modified. This is precisely why tx() isn't supposed to have side effects.

Now I'm not sure quite how transactionality has been implemented by Google. If in fact it fails on the execution of put() if it's going to fail at all, then your approach is quite reasonable.

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: How to tie a Bow Tie
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: how-to-tie-a-bow-tie
CATEGORY: Culture
CATEGORY: Me

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/02/how-to-tie-a-bow-tie.html 
DATE: 02/04/2009 09:31:22 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I recently bought a proper &#39;not tied for you in the factory&#39; bow tie. Just a strip of wavy fabric to be assembled about my own neck! I figured I&#39;d look (even more) dashing with the genuine article, tied just imperfectly enough to make it clear that it&#39;s the real deal. The instructions on the back of the box were frankly useless so I turned to Google and after a few pictorial, textual and video tutorials I got the hang of it:</p><div><div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340105370d5ffc970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BowTieTied" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340105370d5ffc970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340105370d5ffc970b-250wi" style="width: 250px; " /></a>&#0160;<br /></div><br /><div>It&#39;s not easy, and definitely requires a decent understanding of what you&#39;re <span style="font-style: italic;">supposed</span> to do, then some practise until you can actually do it, followed by more practice to fettle it into looking half decent. It&#39;s worth being familiar enough with the procedure to be able to replicate it as necessary, since you may find some joker pulling yours undone at the ball! But my friends would never do that. And I had a backup pre-tied one in my pocket just in case.</div><br /><div>My Marks and Sparks version came with an adjustable clasp in the middle, which is very useful to fit it to the neck properly and allows your hard fought bow creation to be saved for next time.</div><br /><div>So, where are my instructions on how to do it? I shall cheekily defer to those that have gone before me, but humbly suggest that the easiest to follow description, and certainly the one that really sorted me out, can be found here:&#0160;<a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VJv4Qh7zR3E" target="_blank" title="How to tie a bow tie - YouTube">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VJv4Qh7zR3E</a></div><br /><div>Of course the very best thing about a real bow tie is that you can do the &#39;just heading back to the yacht in Monaco harbour for a night cap&#39; look:</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340105370d6190970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="BowTieUntied" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e015888340105370d6190970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e015888340105370d6190970b-250wi" style="width: 250px; " /></a>

TITLE: HTML Colour Codes from Mac OS X Colour Picker
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: html-colour-codes-from-mac-os-x-colour-picker
CATEGORY: Design
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/html-colour-codes-from-mac-os-x-colour-picker.html 
DATE: 01/29/2009 08:57:09 AM
-----
BODY:
<p>I do quite a lot of futzing around developing web apps, and as a result I&#39;m often needing to choose colours to use in them, and I want those colours in HTML hex form - e.g. #ff0000 (<span style="color: #ff0000; font-family: &#39;Trebuchet MS&#39;; ">bright red</span>). The standard Mac OS X colour picker is a pretty reasonable colour picker, with all the features I want: colour wheel, RGB sliders, HSB sliders etc. but it lacks the most crucial feature - easy output of HTML hex codes! This is clearly a criminal omission, and it meant I&#39;d frequently have to convert from RGB 0-255 numbers down to hex, which is tedious in the extreme.</p><br /><div>Hence I was muchly pleased when I found <a href="http://wafflesoftware.net/hexpicker/" target="_blank">Hex Colour Picker</a>, which adds exactly this missing feature to the standard Mac colour picker panel. Perfect. Now I just open TextEdit, hit cmd-shift-c to bring up the colour panel and I have everything I need for web colour picking.</div>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Python Newbie Mistake
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: python-newbie-mistake
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/python-newbie-mistake.html 
DATE: 01/26/2009 10:08:15 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve been banging away at my Google App Engine application. It&#39;ll be a little while yet, but in the meantime, a particular observation about Python.&#0160;I keep getting tripped up when I refer to a member function without the parenthesis. For instance:</p><br /><div>&gt;&gt;&gt; list = [1,2,3,4,5]</div><div>&gt;&gt;&gt; print list.count</div><div>&lt;built-in method count of list object at 0x238fa8&gt;<br /></div><br /><div>This doesn&#39;t print &#39;5&#39;&#0160;because list.count is a reference to the count method itself, which (like everything else in Python) is an object, so gets printed. You need to use list.count() with those important parentheses in order to actually get the answer 5 that you wanted. I keep getting tripped up by this and taking a while to debug the problem every time. Some things like __class__ don&#39;t need parentheses, which I think is part of the confusion. Furthermore, when you use dir() to look up the public features of a class, it doesn&#39;t do anything to show you which ones are functions:</div><br /><div>&gt;&gt;&gt; dir([])</div><div><div>[&#39;__add__&#39;, &#39;__class__&#39;, &#39;__contains__&#39;, &#39;__delattr__&#39;, &#39;__delitem__&#39;, &#39;__delslice__&#39;, &#39;__doc__&#39;, &#39;__eq__&#39;, &#39;__ge__&#39;, &#39;__getattribute__&#39;, &#39;__getitem__&#39;, &#39;__getslice__&#39;, &#39;__gt__&#39;, &#39;__hash__&#39;, &#39;__iadd__&#39;, &#39;__imul__&#39;, &#39;__init__&#39;, &#39;__iter__&#39;, &#39;__le__&#39;, &#39;__len__&#39;, &#39;__lt__&#39;, &#39;__mul__&#39;, &#39;__ne__&#39;, &#39;__new__&#39;, &#39;__reduce__&#39;, &#39;__reduce_ex__&#39;, &#39;__repr__&#39;, &#39;__reversed__&#39;, &#39;__rmul__&#39;, &#39;__setattr__&#39;, &#39;__setitem__&#39;, &#39;__setslice__&#39;, &#39;__str__&#39;, &#39;append&#39;, &#39;count&#39;, &#39;extend&#39;, &#39;index&#39;, &#39;insert&#39;, &#39;pop&#39;, &#39;remove&#39;, &#39;reverse&#39;, &#39;sort&#39;]</div><br /><div>I rather suspect I&#39;ve missed a subtle but important bit of Python understanding, having only scratched the surface so far. If someone can point that out to me I&#39;d be very grateful.&#0160;</div></div>

<p>My MacBook laptop has had to go in for repair as a sliver is splintering off the plastic palm-rest where the lid touches it at the edge. There are also myriad hairline cracks appearing in the bottom shell around the front and back. TITLE: Corned Beef Hash
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: corned-beef-hash
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/corned-beef-hash.html 
DATE: 01/17/2009 09:42:13 PM
-----
BODY:
<iframe frameborder="0" marginheight="0" marginwidth="0" scrolling="no" src="http://rcm-uk.amazon.co.uk/e/cm?t=alwthecri-21&amp;o=2&amp;p=8&amp;l=as1&amp;asins=0716020734&amp;fc1=000000&amp;IS2=1&amp;lt1=_blank&amp;m=amazon&amp;lc1=0000FF&amp;bc1=000000&amp;bg1=FFFFFF&amp;f=ifr" style="float:right;margin-left:10px;margin-bottom:8px;width:120px;height:240px;"></iframe><p>

Another &#39;what I had for dinner&#39; post. Sorry.</p><div><br /><div>Because there&#39;s a recession on, I went for cheap and cheerful: corned beef hash, as described in How to Boil an Egg - the most straightforward no-nonsense cookery book ever and a staple of any kitchen, surely! It really is simple: par-boil diced potato, then throw in a pan with chopped onion and diced corned beef. Fry for fifteen or so minutes with a beaten egg, ketchup, tabasco, worcestershire sauce, seasoning etc. until ready to eat. In my case, serve with baked beans, but I find that if you&#39;re just eating it on its own (like I did with the leftovers next day for lunch) it needs a good splurge of ketchup when served, to lubricate and tastify.</div><br /><div>I found that the corned beef disintegrates from the carefully chopped cubes almost instantaneously. Even my most careful and delicate pushing it around the frying pan resulted in complete disintegration within mere seconds. I&#39;m sure I remember corned beef hash from my childhood having nicely seared cubes of meat, so I don&#39;t know what I did wrong. I can only imagine that I&#39;m remembering wrongly, or I need to buy better ingredients. So I blame Sainsburys in this case.</div><br /><div><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536dcc0e2970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="CornedBeefHash" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834010536dcc0e2970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536dcc0e2970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Python Thoughts
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: python-thoughts
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/python-thoughts.html 
DATE: 01/15/2009 10:53:07 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I&#39;ve spent a bit more time learning Python, because I&#39;ve been dabbling with Google App Engine (which I&#39;m going off rapidly) but mainly because it&#39;s an interesting language. What&#39;s particularly intriguing to me is its similarity to Ruby, and hence where it differs in syntax or approach makes for a notable point of comparison. Of course it&#39;s probably more correct to say that Ruby is similar to Python than the other way around. From my still very small exposure to Python...</p><br /><div>Nice things about Python:</div><div><ul>
<li>Less confusion with the way classes work. Or maybe I just haven&#39;t stumbled into Python&#39;s equivalent of meta-classes and class vs instance variables.</li>
<li>@classmethod is a much neater way of stating what&#39;s a class method rather than an instance method, compared to all that self gubbins, or the dreaded &lt;&lt;.</li>
<li>Optional named arguments for functions, allowing more flexibility for optional arguments and greater clarity when calling. [As an aside I like Objective-C&#39;s way of building argument labels into the method signature, but not it&#39;s square brackety syntax: [obj message:foo]. I&#39;d much rather do obj.message(foo) and in fact with properties in Objective-C 2.0 we see more of this style.]</li>
<li>I think I probably prefer explicit return statements rather than the Ruby way of returning the last evaluated thing in any expression.</li>
<li>List comprehensions. To start with it just seems like a syntactic difference - Python: [x*2 for x in my_list] &#0160;Ruby: my_list.map {|x| x*2}. But Python&#39;s party trick is excluding elements as it goes: [x*2 for x in my_list if x != 3].</li>
</ul>
Nasty things about Python:</div><div><ul>
<li>Seriously - indentation to demarcate blocks? Apparently I&#39;ll get used to it.</li>
<li>Double underscores. __init__ is a complete pain to type. Why not just a single underscore at the start or something?</li>
<li>Fiddly module system. Why the need for an __init__.py file in a directory, just to make it a module? Why the need to explicitly define an __all__ method just to be able to import everything in a module? I want to be able to create a &quot;model/&quot; directory, put all the .py files for my DB classes in there, then import the whole lot from my other classes with ease. When I add a new model class, I shouldn&#39;t have to go and modify the __init__.py file. It seems to be a real pain to split code up into multiple files sensibly in Python. If I&#39;ve missed a trick here - please somebody show me the light!</li>
<li>The string interpolation is OK I suppose, giving the full power of C style formatting, but most of the time you&#39;re just doing simple interpolation and Ruby&#39;s syntax is far more pleasant and readable. Python: &quot;Hello %s, from %s.&quot; % (person, greeter) &#0160;Ruby: &quot;Hello {person}, from {greeter}.&quot; &#0160;Ruby also has a full on formatting system for the few times when you need it.</li>
</ul>
<span>&#0160;</span>&#0160;<br /></div>
-----

<div style="text-align: left;">This is one of those occasions where I had accidentally left the ISO high having been shooting in the dark previously. So this was f8, 1/2000s at ISO 1600, quite unnecessarily!</div><p><br /><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536d1c030970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="LeafDrops" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834010536d1c030970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536d1c030970c-500wi" style="width: 500px; " /></a></p>

And then I went back into the kitchen and discovered the small pile of fresh chopped coriander leaves that I had intended to use as a garnish. TITLE: Getting Twitchy
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: getting-twitchy
CATEGORY: Nature
CATEGORY: Photography

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/getting-twitchy.html 
DATE: 01/11/2009 04:26:18 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>I was stumbling along in the gloom of another grey day at <a href="http://www.watercress-net.org.uk/" target="_blank" title="Watercress Wildlife Association">the WWA</a>. And at 3:30pm it was starting to get dark. Very poor conditions for wildlife photography! Then I noticed a small blue bird on a branch and bingo, there was one of the kingfishers in a spot I&#39;d never seen it before, and handy for me to rest my camera on the rail of a bridge only about 8m away. Here are the best couple of shots I got (the first was actually entirely handheld).</p><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536c5c1dc970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="KingfisherFish 5456" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834010536c5c1dc970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536c5c1dc970c-500wi" style="width: 500px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536bc2f8f970b-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="Kingfisher2 5457" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834010536bc2f8f970b " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536bc2f8f970b-500wi" style="width: 500px; " /></a>
 <br /></div><div style="text-align: center;"><br /></div><div style="text-align: left;">These are the best shots I&#39;ve ever managed to get of kingfishers so I&#39;m very pleased. They&#39;re a long way from decent by any professional standards (the things I see on TV or in magazines make me sick with envy and incredulity) but the opportunity was fleeting and the light dim, with my kit and technique straining at their very limits: 400mm, ISO 3200, f5.6, 1/100s. To top it all, the elusive water rail then walked along beneath it, though I didn&#39;t manage to get a shot of that, and still never have.</div>

ALLOW COMMENTS: 1

CATEGORY: Beer
CATEGORY: Food and Drink

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/beer-review-meantime-london-pale-ale.html 
DATE: 01/10/2009 06:34:10 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>A light but fairly complex taste that&#39;s reasonably bitter and hoppy, with a slightly burnt/roasted edge which I&#39;d only usually expect to fine in dark beers and stouts. The bitterness and 4.3% ABV make it highly quaffable, but maybe it&#39;s a shade too bitter not to get wearing after a while.</p><div><br /><div>Apparently &quot;the International Beer Challenge 2007 awarded Meantime Pale Ale &#39;World&#39;s Top 50 Beers&#39; Status.&quot; I can&#39;t say that I agree with them on this occasion. It&#39;s a quality beer for sure, but not one that will stick in the mind. I can name quite a lot of beers that have blown me away and I remember fondly. This isn&#39;t one of those, though it might be more impressive outdoors on a hot summery day.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536c4025b970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="MeantimePaleAle" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834010536c4025b970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536c4025b970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>

AUTHOR: Sam Carr
TITLE: Google App Engine: First Thoughts
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: google-app-engine-first-thoughts
CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Web/Tech

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/google-app-engine-first-thoughts.html 
DATE: 01/10/2009 12:10:55 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>Having ploughed through much of the documentation, done the tutorial and started writing my own little web app, I have some half-formed thoughts about <a href="http://code.google.com/appengine/" target="_blank">Google App Engine</a> to throw out to the world.</p><div><ul>
<li>As far as I can tell, any sort of data aggregation functionality (counting, averaging etc.) just won&#39;t be possible as the <a href="http://code.google.com/appengine/docs/datastore/" target="_blank">Datastore APIs</a>&#0160;don&#39;t allow for it. I&#39;ve tried to think of ways to fake it but even my most elaborate machinations come up against the buffers. The only way to manage it at all is to do counting and averaging piecemeal, manually keeping the aggregate values you need up to date with each individual entity modification. Unfortunately, that means that you can&#39;t introduce new functionality requiring new aggregate values after you&#39;ve already got a million users, because you&#39;ve missed the chance to record those aggregates along the way.</li>
<li>Python&#39;s OK, but I don&#39;t like using indentation as the sole way to define blocks. But I&#39;m sure I can get used to that.</li>
<li>I really don&#39;t like having to put an empty __init__.py file in any subdirectories of my python code. If I don&#39;t do that it seems I can&#39;t <span style="font-family: Courier;">import foo.bar.Thingy</span>. Breaking up code into multiple files in a sensible directory structure is surely a fairly common thing to do, so I&#39;m amazed that Python makes it strangely difficult. I hope I&#39;ve simply missed something and it&#39;s actually easier than that.</li>
<li>In fact all those double underscores look horrid and are a pain to type. Surely a single underscore would have been quite&#0160;adequate?</li>
<li>The overall experience for learning GAE is very sorted. Smooth and well integrated - all you need to supply is your own decent text editor. I&#39;m trying out TextMate, the darling of Mac OS X code editors, but I&#39;m worried to see that it doesn&#39;t seem to have been updated for over a year.&#0160;</li>
</ul>
</div>
-----

TITLE: Google App Engine Experiments
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1
BASENAME: google-app-engine-experiments

CATEGORY: Programming
CATEGORY: Ruby

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/google-app-engine-experiments.html 
DATE: 01/07/2009 10:24:11 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>It seems to be the done thing these days to learn how to use <a href="http://code.google.com/appengine/" target="_blank">Google App Engine</a> (and thus <a href="http://www.python.org/" target="_blank" title="Python Programming Language">Python</a>) within a couple of hours and then hack out a simple web application to prove how easy it is.</p><br /><div>So with the missus out of town tonight, I&#39;m staying out of trouble by doing just that. I&#39;ve run through the <a href="http://code.google.com/appengine/docs/gettingstarted/" target="_blank">getting started tutorial</a>, which is a delight I have to say, especially because it&#39;s so darned simple to get going on Mac OS X. Python is already installed and there&#39;s a neat app to download from Google which installs the app engine SDK bits and puts a nice GUI front end on it for you to fire up the local test environment and various other handy things, as well as installing the command line tools if you prefer them. Start up TextMate or any other editor of your choice and you&#39;re away!</div><br /><div>You&#39;ll have to take my word for it though, because I&#39;m tired and won&#39;t get onto writing my own app tonight.</div><br /><div>Footnote: Python seems OK, but so far I prefer Ruby, though they seem to share a lot in common.</div>

TITLE: Xmas Tree Takedown
STATUS: Publish
ALLOW COMMENTS: 1
CONVERT BREAKS: wysiwyg
ALLOW PINGS: 1

CATEGORY: Current Affairs
CATEGORY: Me

UNIQUE URL: http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/atc/2009/01/xmas-tree-takedown.html 
DATE: 01/05/2009 10:43:22 PM
-----
BODY:
<p>It only seems like days ago that I put it up, but now the fake Xmas tree must come down again. I always dread this bit as it wrecks my hands trying to get all the fronds folded together into a streamlined torpedo of bristly green foliage. This year I fought back with heavy duty gloves!</p><div><br /><div>Unfortunately I don&#39;t have any gardening gloves, what with not having a garden, so instead I made do with magic potato scrubbing gloves. I can&#39;t say I&#39;ve ever used these for their intended purpose but the idea is that you wear them whilst viciously manhandling potatoes under running water and the dirt and skin just falls off. They&#39;re just as rough on the inside so it&#39;s touch and go whether it&#39;s the potato&#39;s skin or your own that sloughs off. Still, they made a fine job of the Xmas tree, whilst exfoliating my hands beautifully.</div><br /><div style="text-align: center;"><a href="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536b4e6b1970c-popup" onclick="window.open( this.href, &#39;_blank&#39;, &#39;width=640,height=480,scrollbars=no,resizable=no,toolbar=no,directories=no,location=no,menubar=no,status=no,left=0,top=0&#39; ); return false" style="display: inline;"><img alt="XmasTreePotatoGloves" class="at-xid-6a00e5550e01588834010536b4e6b1970c " src="http://alwaysthecritic.typepad.com/.a/6a00e5550e01588834010536b4e6b1970c-400wi" style="width: 400px; " /></a>
